# The Naming

# Chapter 1

The rain found the seams in Suna's shawl before they were halfway up the switchback. Kael walked ahead, his sandals slapping the stone steps with a determination she recognized as bravery; he was eight and trying to look steady for his sisters. Behind her, Deshi climbed with one hand on Peyam's elbow, breathing hard between gusts, and Suna could hear the older woman's complaints building: the mud, the steepness, the weight of the bags they had dragged from the truck before leaving it where the water covered the wheels.

Asha rode on Suna's hip. Four years old and too heavy for this, but the child had stopped walking twenty minutes ago, and there was no fight Suna could win against that kind of exhaustion. Asha's fingers found the wet collar of Suna's blouse and rubbed the fabric between thumb and forefinger. She touched everything. Buttons, bark, wooden spoons, the hems of strangers' clothes when Suna was not fast enough to pull her back. Now she touched the rain, palm open, catching what she could and licking it from her skin.

"Rimi," Suna said. "Stay with Kael."

"I'm with Kael."

"Where I can see you."

Rimi moved one step closer to her brother. She was twelve, angular, already carrying her father's long face, and Suna would have been grateful for the obedience if it had not meant the girl was too frightened to argue.

The shrine came through the rain at the last bend: stone walls the color of wet iron, a covered walkway jutting from the entrance, and behind it the larger compound on the hilltop. Bodies already crowded the overhang. Bags leaned against the wall. A child complained in a bright, relentless voice. Other families had reached shelter first.

Peyam said something to Deshi that the wind took. Deshi answered loudly enough for everyone near them to hear. "Of course there are others. Where else would they go?"

Where else. Below them, where the seven roads should have met, there was only water. Brown and silt-heavy, pushing through the valley with enough force to erase its own edges. Suna could see the roof of a truck at the waterline, a side mirror catching the last dirty light, and a road sign half under. The flood slid into the trees on both sides and vanished around the far bend. Its sound climbed the hill under the rain, steady and pressurized, another weather laid beneath the first.

"Mama," Asha said, and pressed her wet palm to Suna's cheek.

"I know," Suna said. "We're here."

A lean man stood at the mouth of the walkway, rain darkening his shoulders. He did not wave them in. He did not step forward to take a bag. He simply waited until they crossed under the roof and the rain stopped striking their faces.

"How many in the family?" he asked.

Suna set Asha down. Her back flared with relief. "Six. Family name Mera."

"Given names."

"Suna. Rimi. Kael. Asha. Deshi. Peyam."

His eyes moved across them as she spoke, landing on each face in turn. "Your destination before the road closed."

"Gelden. My husband went ahead. We're meeting him." The lie came out flat. She had said it too many times to hear the seam in it.

"I am Olet. Head custodian of this shrine. You will have a place to sleep and food. There are rules. You will hear them when everyone has arrived."

"How many are here?"

"You make seven families."

Seven. Suna looked past him into the hall. Stone floor. Stone walls. A ceiling smoked dark by old lamps. Large enough for the bodies, not for the distance they would want between them. On the far side, a wide arch opened over the valley, and the flood filled it like a second wall. Tree crowns marked the buried roads. The sound came through the arch and settled into the hall. It would be there when the rain eased. It would be there after dark. It was the sound of being kept.

Oil lamps already burned along the walls. An angular woman with rough hands moved from bracket to bracket, tending them with quick economy. She gave Suna one short, measuring look and kept moving. Two younger custodians stacked mats against the west wall and took wet bundles from the newest arrivals without speaking.

Suna counted anyway. Not total. Composition. Children. Elders. Men. Who was sick, who was tired, who had the look of someone who would start trouble when the room got tighter.

Olet kept the threshold moving. The same three questions went to each family. The broad man at the south wall answered "Kondo" with clipped impatience and sent a younger man back into the rain for another sack before Olet had reached the destination question. The thin young father by the entrance said "Parra" so quietly Olet made him repeat it. A man in glasses came up with a baby strapped to his chest, gave "Okoye," and then tried to ask how long the road would stay under water. Olet took the name, not the question.

The biggest family had taken the south side. The broad man with gray at his temples ordered children and sacks out of a puddle while his wife shook rain from an embroidered wedding cloth and folded it over her forearms to save the border work. Near her, an elderly aunt sat on a bundle and rubbed her left ear with her palm in slow circles. A younger man kept one hand at the old woman's shoulder and the other on the stack of bags, ready to move either.

Near the entrance, the Parra man lowered a sleeping girl from his back and settled on the floor with her across his lap. He had one bag. He set it between his feet and curved his body around the child until the rest of the hall had to go around him.

"Suna." Deshi had found a dry strip of floor and was easing herself down with one hand braced against the stone. Peyam bent beside her, careful with his knees, and drew his prayer beads from his pocket as soon as he sat.

"Rimi, Kael, come here," Suna said. "Asha, don't."

Too late. Asha had crossed the wet stones and laid her hand on the wedding cloth, fingers spreading into the heavy embroidery.

The woman holding it looked up first. Her face was tired past annoyance. "Easy, little one."

"That's not ours," Suna said, catching Asha's wrist.

"It's all right," the woman said, but she lifted the cloth higher, out of reach. The broad man glanced over, decided it was not worth his voice, and went back to counting children.

The Okoye family moved in only after Olet had finished with them. The father kept the baby strapped to his chest, the mother pulled a ten-year-old boy through the doorway before he could stop to stare at the flood, and the teenage girl took in the room in one fast sweep and immediately arranged her face into boredom.

The next arrivals came close enough together that the walkway clogged again. One woman kept three children moving with a hand between shoulder blades while the other stopped once beneath the arch and listened to the flood as if it had spoken. Behind them, a man kept the two boys tucked close to his knees while the oldest girl drifted a step ahead and back again. After them came a careful man with his partner, an older woman carrying a canvas bag, and a boy kept so near the adults that his shoulder stayed against somebody's hip. They entered with the concentrated quiet of people used to being noticed for the wrong reasons.

The last family took the longest because the oldest woman would not be hurried. Small, white-haired, exact, she placed each foot and then trusted it. Her son stayed beside her without crowding. A teenage boy hovered behind them with both hands half raised. The girl behind him kept turning to look down the switchback at the drowned road.

By the time the old woman crossed under the roof, the hall held forty-three stranded travelers, their wet bags, their children, their impatience, and the steady rush from the valley below.

***

Olet waited until the path stayed empty. Then he walked to the center of the hall and spoke.

He did not raise his voice. He spoke into the noise and the noise dropped around him.

"This is a transit shrine. It sits at the meeting of seven roads and has served travelers longer than records count. You are welcome here. You will be fed from our stores. You will be given places to sleep. In return, you will follow the shrine's schedule. Meals are communal. Labor is shared. There is a daily gathering. You will hear more tomorrow."

"If the road's under till morning, that's one kind of problem," the man in glasses said. "If it's longer, people need to plan differently. Which is it?"

Olet turned to him. "The road opens when the water drops. The water drops when the rain stops. The rain will stop when it stops."

From the south side came a hard breath that almost passed for a laugh. The broad man in the wedding shirt had folded his arms across his chest and was staring at Olet with the patience already gone out of him.

"Sleeping quarters," Olet said.

He had a system. Suna saw that at once. He did not ask what anyone wanted. He pointed. Families moved.

"The Kondo family. The large room on the south side."

The broad man moved first. At the doorway he stopped and turned back. "My family is twelve. My aunt is seventy-three. We don't put our elders in corners and tell children to make do around them. If this is the Kondo room, I want to hear what provision you're making for us."

"It is the largest room."

"I heard 'largest.' I asked whether the shrine had another arrangement for a family our size."

"It is the largest room available, and it has been assigned to the largest family."

The man held there a moment longer. His wife touched the middle of his back. He went in. The younger man took the old aunt's elbow and helped her over the threshold.

"The Okoye family. The northeast corner of the main hall."

The man in glasses made a small turn of both hands, a private argument gathering itself. His wife looked at the corner and then at the baby. Open on two sides. No door. No wall worth the name. Still, he took the boy's shoulder and led them there. The teenage girl trailed after them with that same practiced indifference, now tighter around the mouth.

"The Mera family. The room beside the kitchen."

"Beside the kitchen?" Deshi said. "So our blankets take smoke all night and by morning everything we own smells like old onions and ash. That's the room?"

"It is what is available."

Deshi looked at Suna as if the answer had come from her. Suna picked up their bags.

"The Parra family. The alcove by the east wall."

The thin young man stood, shifted his daughter onto his hip, and went there without a word. The alcove was barely deep enough for two mats. He set his bag behind the girl as a backstop and sat with one knee up, closing the open side with his body.

"The Tesh family. The west annex."

The exact old woman rose. Her son offered his arm. She took it and moved off at her same measured pace. The teenage boy grabbed the heaviest bag before anyone asked him to.

"The Dao family. The room past the cistern."

The older of the two women counted heads under her breath, touched the younger one's elbow, and steered the children down the passage while the man kept the two boys close beside him. The younger woman gave the flood one last look and followed.

"The Leo family. The alcove near the prayer courtyard."

The careful man did not move immediately. "Is there a curtain there?"

"No."

The man glanced at his partner, at the older woman, at the boy beside them. Then he nodded once and led them toward the courtyard anyway.

"These are your spaces," Olet said. "Arrange them as you wish. The kitchen area is communal. Water is from the cistern. The prayer courtyard is open to all. The custodians' quarters are not. If you need something, ask."

"If the only radio is in your quarters, then the news only comes through you," the man in glasses said from his corner. "That matters when people are deciding what they're walking into."

"There is a radio. It is in the custodians' quarters. We will share what news comes."

"Can we hear it ourselves, then, so we're deciding from the same facts?"

Olet let the question finish. "The custodians will share what news comes."

The man's hands turned again. His daughter watched the exchange with bright, merciless attention. When she caught Suna looking, she dropped her gaze.

Suna carried their bags into the kitchen room. Small. Dry. Cold stone underfoot. A slot of a window opening onto the kitchen overhang, already full of ash smell and old smoke.

"Six of us," Deshi said from the doorway. "In this. You'd call a pantry crowded before you'd call this a room."

"We'll manage." Suna laid out mats from the stack in the hall. Three for the children. One for Deshi. One for Peyam. One for herself. The room was four meters by three, perhaps. With her arms spread she could nearly touch both walls.

"The large family has a room three times this size. They can turn around in there without stepping on somebody's hands."

"They're twelve."

"We're six. That's not a sleeping arrangement. That's storage."

Suna kept counting. Mats. Blankets. Two bags of rice. Dried lentils. Salt. The tin of oil that had leaked in the truck and turned the inside of one bag rancid. If the custodians fed them, it would not matter. If they did not, it would matter fast.

"Peyam needs space for his knees," Deshi said. "He's not a folded blanket you can shove on a shelf. You know that."

"I know." Suna set Peyam's mat along the far wall, where he could stretch out without anyone stepping over him. She took the mat nearest the door. The children would go between her and Deshi. The arrangement settled over the room at once. Six bodies. Kitchen smell. Flood through the window. No date on the end of it.

***

By nightfall the rain still had not let go. It came in long runs, hammering the roof until the lamps thinned, then easing just enough to remind them what quiet was before starting again. The shrine answered with groans from its beams and corridors.

Suna fed her children from their own supplies: rice cooked in the communal kitchen, lentils, a scrap of pickle that Deshi cut into slivers and placed on each mound of rice with medicinal care. The kitchen was the most exposed part of the compound, a stone counter and fire pit under a deep overhang that blocked the worst of the rain but none of the wind. Suna cooked beside Hala from the Dao family; she learned the name from the thirteen-year-old girl who came over to ask Kael if he wanted to see a beetle drowning in a bucket lid. Hala worked fast and without wasted motion. She had done this in bad weather before. They did not talk much, but they made room for each other with glances and brief touches of the hand. When Hala split an onion with the heel of her palm guiding the knife, Suna felt a jolt of recognition. Her own mother had cut onions that way.

In the prayer courtyard, open to the sky, Peyam stood barefoot on the wet stone and prayed. Suna could see him from the kitchen overhang: thin back straight, beads hanging from one hand, rain running down his face. He had not asked whether prayer was allowed. He had found the courtyard, taken off his sandals, and begun. When he finished, he lifted his face into the rain for one quiet breath, then put his sandals back on and came inside.

After dinner, if you could call it that, Suna washed the children's faces and hands at the cistern. Kael submitted. Rimi turned her back to the room and did it herself, guarding the small privacy she had begun claiming six months ago. Asha made the washing into another investigation. She rubbed Suna's wet wrist. She pressed her palm to the cool stone rim. She tasted the water.

"Don't drink that, Asha."

Suna was wringing out the cloth when Asha reached past her and touched the face of a boy waiting his turn.

He belonged to the wedding family, nine or ten perhaps, round-cheeked, still rubbing at a mark on his collar. He blinked in surprise and then stood still for it.

"Sorry," Suna said, catching Asha's wrist. "She does that."

"It's fine." The boy touched his own cheek once, curious now, and went back to his people.

Asha watched him leave with her hand still open, fingers curled around nothing.

Their room was lamplight, stone, and six people packed tighter than sleep allowed. Peyam fell asleep almost as soon as he lay down. His beads clicked once and then stopped. Deshi turned her back to the room, though Suna could hear from her breathing that she was still awake.

Suna settled the children. Kael on his back, knees up, his usual position since he was three. Rimi on her side facing the wall, breathing too evenly. Asha last. Even with her eyes closed, her hands kept moving over the mat, the blanket seam, the weave of the cotton. She found Suna's sleeve and worried the hem between thumb and forefinger until her breathing lengthened. The fingers slowed, paused, then searched once more before finally going still.

Suna lay on her back and did not sleep.

The compound was louder now that everyone was trying to be quiet. The wedding patriarch talking low and hard in his room. The baby crying because the air, the stone, the people, all of it smelled wrong. Melie's father murmuring to her in the east alcove. The older Tesh woman coughing once. Bare feet moving through the corridor.

And underneath everything, the flood, filling the silences from below with patient weight.

"Mama." Kael was nearly asleep, voice gone small. "When are we going to Papa?"

"When the road opens."

"When does the road open?"

"When the water goes down."

"When does the—"

"Sleep, Kael."

He did. Children could do that. They could take an answer with holes in it and drift anyway. Rimi stayed awake beside him. Suna could tell from the held steadiness of her breathing. Twelve years old and already rehearsing calm for the people around her. She had her father's long face and, Suna feared, his talent for silence that meant nothing good.

Suna pressed her hand to the floor beside the mat. Cold stone. Clean indifference. She kept her palm there until the cold climbed her wrist.

In the morning, or in two days, or whenever the rain stopped and the roads came back out of the flood, she would take the children to Gelden. She would go to her husband's family and explain. That was the plan. It had been the plan since he left.

Not since he went ahead. Since he left.

The fact sat in her body like swallowed metal. He had not gone to prepare the house or find work. He had packed a bag while the children were at school and Deshi was at the market and Peyam was praying. He had taken his good shoes and the money from the tin above the stove. He had left a note that said *I'm sorry, I can't do this.* Suna had read it, folded it down to a square no bigger than a stamp, put it in her pocket, and gone to collect the children from school. She had cooked dinner. That evening she had told Deshi that her son had gone ahead to Gelden to prepare. Deshi had looked at her, unreadable, and said, "Without telling me?"

"It was sudden," Suna had answered. "He wanted to get ahead of the rains."

Three months. Three months of sudden. Three months of Kael asking when they were going to Papa and Suna saying soon. Three months of Rimi watching her with that old, evaluating face. Three months of Deshi's questions wearing at the walls of the lie. She had kept a marriage standing by habit and grammar. She had pointed the children toward a man who was nowhere at the end of the road.

Now the road itself was gone. The shrine held thirty-seven strangers around her, plus her own five. Olet had given them a room the size of an animal stall and spoken with the finality of a man who had watched too many families come apart indoors. At the far end of this journey, whenever the flood released it, no husband would be waiting. There would only be his family's house and their faces when they understood what he had done.

Panic never came as a wave. It came as a list. What she owed. What she could not pay. Rice for maybe four days if she stretched it. Kael's sandals thinning at the sole. Rimi's silence getting longer. Asha on other people's things, other people's faces. Peyam's knees. Deshi's questions. The lie about the husband. The money running out. The road under water. The note in the bag. She still carried it in the inner pocket with the children's things, and sometimes she touched the folded square the way Peyam touched his first bead, not from hope, just to confirm the damage was still there.

Somewhere in the compound, a woman started humming. The melody was low and repetitive, turning back on itself until it settled into the air. It was for another child in another room. Even so, Suna listened. Beneath the panic, beneath the lie worn smooth by use, another feeling rose and embarrassed her with its force: relief. Not safety. Nothing that generous. But shelter. Walls. Other adults awake in the dark, listening for their own children. For one night, at least, she was held inside the same fear as everyone else.

Asha's hand found hers. Small, warm, damp from sleep. The child's fingers closed around two of Suna's and rested there, reading even this.

The rain hammered the shrine. The flood kept talking in the valley. Suna closed her eyes and did not sleep, and outside the roads stayed gone.


# Chapter 2

Fasa was wiping the lamp ledges again when the hall began to lighten, weak brass daylight pushing through the open side after a night of rain. She moved from ledge to ledge with a cloth, though the stone where the oil lamps would sit had been wiped yesterday and the day before that and every day for twenty years. The work was not cleaning. It was attention. Prayer required it. Diagnosis required it. So did any encounter that meant to get at the truth instead of merely passing through the motions of asking.

She set the cloth down and crouched over the first lamp. The wick was trimmed. The oil sat just below half. Her hands knew the sequence, which left her mind free for what it had been doing since yesterday's arrivals: sorting shapes.

Seven families. Forty-three people in Olet's ledger. They had come up the switchback through rain, carrying bundles wrapped in feed sacks, sleeping children, tin pots, one birdcage without a bird. Fasa had watched from the hall while Olet and the assistants received them below. She never went down to greet arrivals. Receiving belonged to Olet. Naming belonged to her.

What she had, before anyone spoke in the hall, were impressions only. The Kondos moved like a practiced column and took up space without apology. Suna Mera kept three children and two elders in motion with one pair of hands. Jin Parra carried his daughter as if the compound might try to keep her. Bram Okoye watched the walls, the exits, the custodians. Gitta Tesh answered registration questions before Olet had finished asking them. Among the Daos, Hala did the speaking, Bett none of it, and a man Hala called Kal kept two boys tucked close to his knees. The Leos formed a small square around their son and held it even while Cade struggled with the form. That was enough to begin with.

She straightened and set the last lamp in place. The floor had been swept. The benches made a loose half-circle around the worn patch of stone where she would stand. Beyond the open wall, the valley was still brown water and drowned road. The flood kept up its low, steady rush beneath the morning noises of the shrine. People stopped hearing it after an hour. Their bodies did not.

She had been wrong before. She had taken reserve for deceit, grief for calculation, composure for innocence. Twenty years of namings had not given her certainty. They had given her the discipline to keep looking after first impressions curdled or softened or proved false.

Olet's step sounded behind her, weight falling harder on the right foot as always.

"Are they ready?" she asked.

"They are here," he said. "I will ring the bell when you wish."

"What do you make of them?"

"Seven families stopped by water." His pauses never felt like hesitation. "The rest is your work."

He waited in the doorway. He could wait longer than anyone Fasa knew. The consistency of it had unsettled her once. Now it felt like weather.

The bell, when it rang, would not sound like invitation. Rice was already steaming in the kitchen. Children could smell it. Adults could smell it too. Fasa believed in the naming with the full stubborn weight of her twenty years, and belief did not make the bargain kinder. The road brought people here. The shrine put a question between them and breakfast and made them answer it in front of strangers.

She did not think the leverage was the heart of the rite. The heart was witness: the way a family arranged its own story once hunger, shame, pride, and other people's eyes were all in the room together. That was where the gaps showed. That was where language stiffened or thinned. That was where she learned what to return to later.

"Ring it," she said.

---

Olet struck the bell three times. The iron note carried through the kitchen overhang, past the cistern, into the sleeping rooms where mats were still warm from bodies. The sound cut the morning in two.

They entered with the wary order of people stepping into a clinic or a courtroom.

The Kondos came first, all twelve of them, Venn leading and Tila counting under her breath as they crossed the threshold. Mava lowered herself to the bench nearest the open wall and set her palm circling against her left ear at once. Arash lingered half a stride out of line, not enough for anyone to call it defiance, enough for Fasa to mark it.

The Meras followed close behind. Suna carried Asha on one hip and kept the other two children moving with a touch to the shoulder, a nudge at the back. Deshi entered with her chin up and Peyam with his prayer cap in both hands, turning it once before he sat.

The Okoyes came in a tight knot: Bram ahead, Nessa with baby Ada pressed high against her chest, Tomas looking from adult to adult, Leni already choosing the place nearest the door.

Jin Parra brought Melie in by the hand and did not look at anyone long enough to invite a greeting.

The Teshes were quiet enough that Kiri's sandal scraping stone sounded loud. Miro guided his family to the far bench. Enn kept hold of her braid. Fen stood until Gitta chose her seat, then sat only when she did.

The Daos filled more space than Fasa had expected at first glance. Hala entered with Raya and Sem. Kal came behind with his wife and their two boys, both children thin-legged and solemn. Bett was last, Nori at her side, the space between their bodies so exact it looked measured.

The Leos came last of all. Cade held Pik's hand. Wynn carried the canvas bag with the day's necessities. Vera followed them with her mouth already set against the room.

Forty-three family members in the hall, five custodians against the walls and doorway, rain beginning again outside. The lamps stayed dark; daylight was harsher than flame and better for seeing what people failed to hide.

Fasa stepped onto the worn patch of stone at the center. The room settled around her in small noises: Ada's wet breathing against Nessa's shoulder, a shoe scraping, the first hard taps of rain on the overhang, the flood beneath it all.

"My name is Fasa," she said. "I keep the naming at this shrine. Olet oversees the shrine's daily life. This part is mine."

The word mine was never ownership. It was burden.

"If you stay here longer than a night, you name yourselves. Your family name. Your number. The relations you claim. The road you came by and the road you mean to take when the water gives it back. One voice may speak for a family, or several may. I will ask when I need more. Say only what you will stand behind tomorrow."

Nobody shifted at the last sentence. The stillness in the room changed anyway.

"Who will begin?"

Venn Kondo was on his feet before the question had fully left her mouth. He planted himself as if the hall had been built to receive exactly this posture.

"The Kondo family stands twelve," he said. "Venn Kondo speaking. My wife Tila. My son Arash. My sister Habel and her daughter Ori. My aunt Mava. My brother Pol's house with mine. We are bound for Tessar. My son's wedding waits there."

At the word wedding, Arash's mouth flattened. His gaze stayed on the open wall and the rain beyond it.

"All traveling together for the marriage?" Fasa asked.

"All of us," Venn said.

He sat with the measured control of a man paying a tax he disliked and expected to be done with.

Suna Mera stood next. Asha reached for her skirt, and Deshi peeled the child's fingers loose without looking down.

"Suna Mera. Six. My mother-in-law Deshi. Her brother Peyam. My daughter Rimi. My son Kael. My youngest, Asha. We're headed south, to my sister's town."

"And your husband?"

Suna's hands locked together so tightly the cords stood out under the skin. Rimi's head came up. Kael stopped swinging his foot.

"He's ahead of us," Suna said. "We're meeting him there."

Deshi did not turn toward her. The old woman's stillness had the flat force of a slap.

"Thank you," Fasa said.

Bram Okoye rose before she called the next name. He stood as if the room contained students who might yet be persuaded.

"Bram Okoye. Five. My wife Nessa. My daughter Leni. My son Tomas. The baby is Ada."

"Where have you come from?"

"The capital." He drew breath through his nose. "There are checkpoint tables on the eastern roads now. Household declarations. Papers. Categories. We took the mountain road instead."

Nessa tightened her hold on Ada. Leni bent over her bitten thumbnail and worried it until the skin reddened.

"You taught?" Fasa asked.

"I did."

The past tense landed harder than the rest of his answer.

Jin Parra did not stand. He sat forward with both hands on his knees and spoke as if every extra word cost him something material.

"Jin Parra. Two. Me and my daughter, Melie."

Fasa waited.

"We are going north," he said after a moment. "To her mother's family."

"And her mother?"

His jaw seized hard enough that she heard the click when it let go.

"She died."

Three adults looked toward the child on the floor before they caught themselves. Melie kept braiding a bit of frayed string she had found between the stones.

"When?" Fasa asked.

"Three months ago."

"Thank you."

He nodded once, not gratitude, only acknowledgment that the required wound had been exposed and could be covered again.

Miro Tesh rose with one hand braced on the bench behind him.

"Miro Tesh. Five. My wife Enn. My daughter Kiri. Fen. My mother, Gitta. We were on our way to a burial in the valley before the road washed out."

Enn had wound her braid around her fist by the time he finished.

"And Fen?" Fasa asked.

Miro's glance went to his mother.

"He was raised under my roof."

Gitta lifted her face a fraction.

"Fen is my son's son," she said.

The sentence arrived clean. No drag, no excess, no tremor in it anywhere.

"Thank you," Fasa said.

Hala Dao stood with the abruptness of someone stepping between a blow and the body meant to take it. Kal shifted as if to stand beside her, but a single look from Hala kept him seated.

"Dao family. Nine. Me. My children Raya and Sem. My brother Kal, his wife, and their two boys. My sister Bett. Nori, Bett's girl. We're headed for the coast."

"And your husband?" Fasa asked.

"I'm not married."

Fasa turned to Bett. "And you?"

Bett met her eyes and kept her mouth shut.

Nori pressed both palms between her knees. One of Kal's boys whispered something to his mother and got hushed at once.

"Bett isn't married either," Hala said.

The words cost her. Fasa could hear it.

"Thank you," she said.

Cade Leo stood last. Wynn's thumb touched the inside of his wrist for a second and disappeared.

"Cade Leo. Four. Wynn is my partner. Pik is our son. Vera is my mother." He swallowed. "We left because the new forms want a family shaped one way, and we aren't shaped that way."

The word partner hit the room and stayed there. Deshi's mouth sharpened. Vera looked straight back at her until the expression went blank again.

"Pik starts school in the autumn," Wynn said. "We need to be settled before then."

"And the boy?" Fasa asked. "By birth, or adoption?"

Cade's answer came rougher than the rest. "He's ours. That's the true part."

Vera put her hand on Pik's head, not soothing him, staking ground.

"All right," Fasa said. "The first naming is heard."

The room shifted as soon as she said it. Before the bell, each family had kept its arrangement inside its own sleeping space. Now the arrangements sat in open air where forty-two other people could measure them.

Kael had heard his mother place an absent man ahead of them on the road and say it without blinking. Arash had heard his father bind him to a wedding before strangers. Bram had brought the state's language into the shrine's hall and made the likeness plain enough to sting. Jin had forced death past his teeth with his daughter three arm lengths away. Hala had spent her own breath covering Bett's refusal. Cade had offered the room a word for his family and watched the room weigh it. And Gitta had laid down a lie so smoothly it almost vanished into the stone.

This was what Fasa trusted in the naming. Not mercy. Not comfort. Pressure. Put people under witness, make them say who they were while food smoked from the kitchen and rain sealed the road and strangers listened hard enough to remember. The truth did not leap out whole. First came the strain around it: the child who looked up too fast, the partner whose hand flashed out and back, the old woman whose answer slid into place with no scrape at all.

Olet rang the bell once from the doorway to release them to the morning meal. Children moved first, hungry and relieved. Adults followed more slowly, sorting themselves back into units, each family reassembling around the version of itself it had just spoken aloud.

Jin was the last to leave. He held out his hand without speaking. Melie took it and came with him, the string braid dangling from her fist. He did not look back.

Fasa stayed where she was. Rain blurred the valley. The flood below remained brown, patient, and entirely unimpressed by what had been said in its hearing.

The next naming would not ask for more names. It would ask, meal by meal and morning by morning, whether the names already spoken could bear weight. Gossip would press against them. Children's attachments, children's refusals, ordinary hours in close quarters would press until some claims held and others split along the grain she had glimpsed this morning.

She folded the cloth. She pinched out the one lamp she had lit. Then she went toward the kitchen, where the smell of rice had thickened and forty-three people were about to take their first meal under the force of what they had said.


# Chapter 3

The bell hit the sleeping quarters like somebody throwing iron at stone. Leni dragged the pillow over her head and thought, with feeling, that four mornings at the shrine was already too many.

It wasn't a gentle bell or a good-morning-honored-guests bell. It was a flat iron clang that one of the custodians' assistants struck from the prayer courtyard at what had to be an hour no reasonable person would voluntarily inhabit. The assistant was a young woman with close-cropped hair and forearms like she split wood for a living, which, Leni had learned, she did. She rang the bell with the same expression every time — not unkindness, not warmth, just the face of a person performing a task that existed before she was born and would continue after she was dead.

Leni pulled her knees to her chest on the sleeping mat and pressed her face into the thin pillow, which smelled like stone dust and other people's hair, and listened to the compound wake up.

Forty-three people waking up sounds like this: baby Ada crying first, because Ada cried at everything, because Ada was one year old and that was her entire skill set. Then Nessa's quiet murmuring as she lifted Ada against her chest. Then the Kondo family — all twelve of them — rustling and murmuring in their corner like a single organism with too many limbs. One of the younger Kondo boys always coughed first thing, a dry bark that echoed off the stone walls and made someone else's child whine about being cold. Tomas turned over on his mat and bumped his knee against Leni's ankle, and she shoved his leg away without opening her eyes. The flood rose steadily beneath everything, that low permanent rush from the valley floor, like the hill itself was clearing its throat.

Bram was already sitting up. Leni could tell from the direction of his breathing — he breathed differently when he was upright, a teacher's posture reasserting itself even in the dark. He reached over Tomas and touched Leni's shoulder.

"Up," he said.

"Why."

"Because the bell rang."

"The bell's not for us. It's for people who want to pray."

"The bell's for everyone. Get up."

Leni opened her eyes. The sleeping quarters were dim — oil lamplight from the corridor, gray monsoon dawn barely reaching the high stone windows. Bodies lay in rows on mats, and the whole room had the intimate strangeness of sleeping near strangers. Across the partition, she could hear Deshi Mera telling Suna something in a voice that suggested Suna was doing it wrong, whatever it was. On the other side, one of the Kondo cousins was trying to fold his blanket while his mother corrected his corners.

She sat up, pulled her braids off her face, and bit the edge of her thumbnail before she caught herself doing it.

Morning prayers were in the courtyard, and by Day 4 Leni knew the cast well enough to count the absences before she was fully awake. Olet stood under the overhang. Fasa stood beside him with her hands folded and her face set in that workmanship expression, like she was checking the joints on a table nobody else could see wobbling. Peyam Mera was already there, small and precise, lips moving with words he somehow seemed to know. Jin Parra waited at the edge, not praying, just standing through the hour because the courtyard cost him less than lying awake beside Melie. Venn Kondo still didn't come. Tila did. Arash had shown up once, the morning before, and not returned. Bram had tried prayers on Day 3 and reported back to Nessa that they were "interesting from an anthropological perspective." He hadn't gone back. The Leos stayed away entirely.

After prayers the whole compound jammed itself onto the cistern steps. The stone was slick, the air smelled of wet moss and metal, and everybody needed water at once. Venn announced that the Kondos needed three jars before anybody else because twelve people drank more than four. Tila didn't argue with him. She just started moving bodies. She put the older children on the steepest stretch and the younger ones at the landing. Filled jars passed hand to hand instead of carried whole. When Suna Mera came up with two empty pots because breakfast was on her, Tila took the first full jar and put it straight into Suna's hands. Even Venn didn't object.

By the time Leni got back to the kitchen overhang with the onion basket, she'd identified the compound's real hierarchy, and it had almost nothing to do with who sounded important.

Day 4's cooking pair was the Meras and the Okoyes. Suna was already at the fire, rice soaking, flatbread dough under her palms, dal thickening in a pot she'd gotten started before the bell. Nessa arrived with Ada on her hip and a stack of bowls she nearly dropped when one of the Kondo boys cut behind her. Suna caught the top bowl without turning.

"Left side," she said. "Those stack easier."

Nessa shifted obediently. "Thanks."

Deshi took up position behind Suna like weather.

"Is that how much salt you're using?"

"Yes."

"For this many people."

"Yes, Deshi."

"Your mother, god rest her, always said salt is cheaper than complaint. But then she was cooking for four."

Suna added more salt. She did it without expression, which Leni respected. The trick with adults like Deshi wasn't winning. It was denying them the fight.

"Your mother's helping," Suna said to Leni as Nessa tried again with the bowls. It wasn't a request so much as a practical redeployment.

Leni sat on an upturned crate near the edge of the overhang, where the rain came down in a silver curtain two feet from her knees, and started peeling onions. The stone under her feet still held yesterday's heat. Smoke from the fire crawled into her hair and eyes. Jin came up from the cistern with another jar braced against his hip, shoulders rounded forward as if he'd agreed long ago to carry whatever anybody handed him. Melie trailed him, leaned against his leg when he set the water down, then wandered back toward the courtyard before he could look at her for too long.

Across the yard, Miro and Enn Tesh were already headed for the latrines with rolled sleeves and the expression of people who understood that life was mostly maintenance. Fen carried the sloshing bucket behind them, taller than Leni, dark hair in his eyes, moving with the specific resentment of a sixteen-year-old being told the disgusting job built character. He passed the kitchen, caught her looking, and she jerked her gaze down hard enough to nick her thumb with the peeling knife.

It bled fast. She shoved the thumb into her mouth and tasted iron under the onion. Nessa was beside her a second later, Ada still on her hip.

"Careful. Let me see."

"It's fine, Mom."

"Show me."

Leni held up her hand. Nessa peered at the cut as if the knife might've introduced a whole new plague to the shrine.

"Keep it clean," Nessa said. "We don't know what's on these surfaces."

"It's an onion, not a biohazard."

"Leni."

"I'm keeping it clean."

Nessa went back to the bowls. Her mother's fear was always looking for an object it could justify itself with. This morning it had chosen Leni's thumb.

Breakfast happened in a rush because forty-three people waiting for food turned even the quiet families into weather. Plates passed hand to hand. The Kondos took their usual corner and made a wall out of themselves. The Daos sat close enough to the kitchen that Hala could intercept anyone who approached Bett. The Leos made a small careful cluster around Pik. The shrine kept sorting itself into units, then leaking around the edges anyway.

When the last plate was scraped mostly clean, Olet stepped in and assigned labor with the same flat calm he brought to everything.

"The Parras and the Teshes will take the terraced garden this morning," he said. "The weeds have grown through the herb rows. The Daos will clean the main hall. The Okoyes and the Leos will gather firewood from the north slope — the fallen branches are dry enough."

"The Kondos?" Venn asked from his corner.

"The Kondos took the latrine yesterday, and the Meras cooked. They may rest this morning."

Venn accepted that with a single nod. Arash didn't. He was on his feet before Leni had swallowed her last bite.

"I'll help with the garden," Arash said.

Venn looked at him. Two bowls stopped halfway to mouths.

"I'd rather move than sit," Arash said, looking at the hall instead of at his father.

That hit harder than yelling would've.

Bram leaned toward Leni. "Ready for firewood?"

"Sure. Is there anything more thrilling than wet branches?"

"On a hilltop shrine in a monsoon? Probably not."

They gathered firewood on the north slope — Leni, Bram, Cade Leo, and Wynn Leo. The slope was steep and wet, and the fallen branches were heavy with rain. Cade moved carefully, testing each foothold before committing his weight, and Wynn walked ahead with the confidence of someone who didn't worry about falling. They worked in near-silence. Leni noticed that Cade and Wynn communicated in small touches — a hand on the shoulder to indicate a branch worth collecting, a tap on the forearm when the slope turned slippery. They had their own language, built from years of reading each other's bodies, and it was the opposite of how the naming wanted families to speak: no declarations, no formal categories, just a hand finding the right shoulder at the right time.

From the slope, Leni could see down to the terraced garden where Jin and Miro Tesh worked side by side, pulling weeds in the rain. Arash had joined them. Jin worked steadily, bending and pulling without pause, because the alternative was sitting with his own thoughts. Miro matched his pace. Arash was faster but less systematic, yanking weeds in clumps and leaving roots behind. Enn Tesh knelt at the far end of the terrace, replanting herb seedlings the rain had displaced, her fingers pressing the soil around each stem with the care of someone who knew food grown here meant food that didn't come from the custodians' stores.

By noon on Day 4, the shrine smelled of wet wood, cooking smoke, and forty-three bodies that hadn't had a proper bath since arriving. The rain had paused, which didn't mean it was over — the clouds sat low and fat over the valley like a held breath. From the main hall's open side, the flood was visible below: brown water covering the valley floor, the switchback road drowned past the third bend. The roads that had brought them here were still gone.

Leni looked at that water and thought: we're not going anywhere.

And it occurred to her — looking at who had cooked, who had hauled water, who had cleaned, who had pulled weeds — that the compound was already being run by the people who were useful, not the people who were loud. Suna's cooking gave her a say in how meals worked that Deshi's criticism couldn't touch. Jin's labor earned him a nod from Olet that Venn's resting did not. Tila organized the Kondos with more practical authority than Venn's pronouncements. And Enn Tesh, replanting herbs nobody asked her to replant, had just done more for the collective food supply than anyone who'd spent the morning asserting their rights.

Venn still spoke for the Kondos. Bram still framed arguments that nobody could answer. But the shrine didn't run on speeches. It ran on hands.

---

The children figured it out before the adults did.

Leni watched it happen from the courtyard steps, where she sat with her journal balanced on her knees, because the courtyard was the only place where she could be alone without being alone — people passed through, but nobody stopped to supervise her.

It started with Asha. Suna's youngest was four, and four-year-olds don't have politics. Asha had spent the morning following Suna around the kitchen, getting underfoot, being redirected, and finally being deposited in the courtyard with a firmness that suggested Suna's patience had a floor. Asha sat on the warm stone for exactly thirty seconds before she walked over to where Pik Leo was playing with a pile of smooth river stones he'd found near the cistern.

Pik looked up, shoved the flattest stones toward her, and Asha sat down next to him. That was apparently enough.

Then Melie Parra drifted over. She stopped beside them, eyes flicking from Pik to Asha to the tower. Three seconds, maybe. Then she sat and started picking through the pile for the smoothest stones.

Nori Dao came next — Bett's daughter, seven, with the same economy of movement Leni had noticed in Bett, everything purposeful, nothing wasted. She didn't ask to join. She brought a stone and placed it on top of the tower and that was that.

Kael Mera, eight, arrived running from the direction of the sleeping quarters, chased by Kiri Tesh, also eight, both of them making a noise that suggested they had decided a stone courtyard was a racetrack. They nearly knocked over the tower. Asha said something sharp — Leni couldn't hear the words, but the tone was pure Deshi, three generations distilled into a four-year-old's voice — and Kael stopped, looked, and sat down.

By the middle of the afternoon, the courtyard held a cluster of children that made no sense by the shrine's family map. Asha Mera, Melie Parra, Pik Leo, Nori Dao, Kael Mera, Kiri Tesh, Ori Kondo, two of the younger Kondo cousins, and Tomas — Leni's own brother, who had abandoned her to join the group with a ten-year-old's confidence that playing was more important than family loyalty.

The stone tower had evolved into a game Leni didn't recognize — partly building, partly tag, partly an elaborate system of rules that the children were inventing in real time and revising by shouting. Pik was the most inventive — he kept proposing modifications that the others either adopted or ignored, and he seemed equally happy either way, his face lit up — a child who had discovered other children after being, Leni guessed, often the only child in whatever room he was in. Ori Kondo was the enforcer — she had inherited her uncle Venn's sense of order, if not his rigidity, and she settled disputes with a firmness that made the younger children comply without resentment. Tomas, who at home would never have voluntarily spent time with anyone under the age of nine, was teaching Kiri a clapping game that involved increasingly complicated hand patterns and both of them failing and laughing.

Rimi Mera, twelve, sat at the edge of the group, old enough to feel the pull of both sides. She braided Ori's hair while Ori argued with Tomas about whose turn it was, and her fingers moved with the automatic skill of a girl who'd been braiding hair since she was old enough to reach. Raya Dao, thirteen, leaned against the courtyard wall with her arms crossed, watching the younger children with an expression that said she was absolutely not playing, she was just standing here, it had nothing to do with the game. Sem Dao, eleven, had no such dignity — he was in the middle of it, building and arguing and laughing, a lanky boy with a laugh that started in his stomach and didn't stop until someone pushed him over, which Kael did twice.

Leni watched. She was too old for this. Too old by exactly the right amount to make it painful, because part of her wanted to sit down and stack stones with Asha, and the rest of her knew that wanting that was itself the proof she was past it.

She bit her thumbnail and wrote in her journal: *The kids don't care whose kids they are.*

Fen Tesh was on the far side of the courtyard, sitting on the low wall that separated the prayer space from the sleeping quarters. He had a stick and was scraping patterns into the moss between the stones — not watching the children, not ignoring them, occupying the same in-between space Leni was in. The other teenager. She thought about going over and saying something, but what would she say? Nice shrine. Great flood. Love what they've done with the mandatory prayers. She stayed where she was.

The parents cared. Suna came to the courtyard door and stood there for a long moment, her eyes tracking Asha, Kael, Rimi — counting her children, placing them in the geography of the group. Her face stayed calm, but her fingers worried at each other as if she were sorting invisible tasks. Asha was sitting in Ori Kondo's lap while Ori braided a piece of string into something. Suna watched this and then turned back to the kitchen without saying anything, but Leni could see the effort of it — the deliberate decision not to call Asha back, not to draw a line, because drawing a line would require explaining where the line was and why.

Nessa appeared later with Ada fussing on her hip. She found Tomas in the scrum and relaxed visibly — Leni could see the tension leave her mother's shoulders when she confirmed all three of her older children were accounted for. Baby Ada reached toward the group with both fists and made a sound of longing that no one over the age of one could have produced without embarrassment. Nessa bounced her and retreated.

Jin Parra came to the courtyard and leaned against the wall, watching Melie play. He stood exactly far enough away that she wouldn't notice him watching, and his face held an expression Leni associated with people trying to memorize something. He rubbed the side of his jaw — that thing he did, the brief lock before his mouth opened, like speech was a door that stuck. After a few minutes, Melie saw him and waved, and he waved back, and she turned back to the game. He stayed.

Venn Kondo sent Tila to collect the Kondo children for afternoon rest, and Tila came to the courtyard with the authority of her voice alone. "Ori. The others. Come rest." The Kondo cousins peeled off from the group without argument, but Ori looked up from the string she was braiding and said, "Five more minutes."

Tila waited.

"Please."

Tila gave her five minutes. Leni timed it in her head. At four minutes and thirty seconds, Ori kissed the top of Asha's head — an odd gesture, casual and intimate, from one child to another child she'd known for barely four days — and followed Tila back inside.

The play group shrank but didn't dissolve. It kept reforming throughout the afternoon in different configurations, like water finding its level. The children moved between families without asking permission. They shared food, shared space, shared the game whose rules mutated hourly. By late afternoon, Melie was asleep with her head on Nori's stomach in the prayer courtyard, both of them curled on the warm stone like animals who'd chosen each other's warmth by instinct, and Kiri Tesh was trying to teach Tomas a song in a melody Leni didn't recognize — something pentatonic and haunting that sounded like it came from somewhere far away.

None of this was organized. None of it was sanctioned. The adults saw it happening and didn't stop it, because stopping it would require explaining why, and none of them had an explanation that didn't sound insane or cruel. *You can't play with her because she's a Kondo. You can't sit with him because he's a Parra.* Even Venn, who had the most rigid sense of family boundary, didn't say it outright. He sent Tila to enforce rest time and hoped proximity would create the distance he couldn't bring himself to name.

It didn't.

---

By evening on Day 4, Leni knew two things about the custodians' quarters: the radio was in there, and the families weren't supposed to linger outside the door. Nobody had actually said they couldn't listen. That was different. She'd figured that out on Day 2.

Now heads were turning toward the custodians' door every time it opened. Whatever was coming over the radio, it wasn't good.

The evening gathering was the custodians' final scheduled event. After the dinner meal, the families collected in the main hall for what Fasa called "reflection" and what Leni called sitting around while adults talked about nothing useful. Oil lamps threw their warm unsteady light across the stone walls. Moths the size of Leni's thumb circled the flames, casting shadows that pulsed and shrank. The evening air was heavy, saturated with the smell of rain and wet earth and the woodsmoke that permeated everything — hair, clothes, skin. By the fourth day, everyone at the shrine smelled the same. Like the shrine.

Leni was bracing for another round of adults talking in circles when Peyam Mera produced a deck of cards.

Leni didn't know where the cards came from. They were old, soft-cornered, played with for decades until the faces were blurred and the suits were guesswork. Peyam had been one of the quietest people at the shrine since they arrived — quieter even than Jin, and Jin had turned silence into a vocation. Peyam was small and deliberate in his movements, with his carefully trimmed white beard and his thread bracelet and his genuine absorption in Olet's prayers. His lips moved at the edge of everything else he did, so steady Leni sometimes stopped noticing them.

But he was also, it turned out, a card player.

"You play?" he said to Mava Kondo. He threw it across the room at full courtyard volume, and Mava looked up from the corner where she'd been sitting with her knees under a blanket and her left hand rubbing her ear in that slow circle Leni had already come to associate with her.

"I play," Mava said. Seventy-three, white-haired, frail enough that the wind through the open side of the hall seemed to push her around. But her eyes, when she said it, were sharp as glass.

They sat cross-legged on Peyam's mat, the cards between them, and Peyam dealt. The game was one Leni didn't recognize — a regional variation, something with trumps and tricks and a complicated bidding system that involved tapping the mat with different knuckles.

The first hand went quickly. Mava won. Peyam studied his remaining cards and said nothing.

The second hand went slower. Peyam bid high. Mava matched him. When the tricks were played out, Peyam turned over his final card with a small flourish and smiled — the first smile Leni had seen from him that wasn't directed at god.

Then Mava turned over hers.

"That's not a legal play," Peyam said.

"It is if you're playing hill rules."

"There are no hill rules."

"There are hill rules. My mother taught me, and her mother taught her, and I've been playing hill rules since before your beard came in."

"You can't trump a trump with a lesser suit. It's not done."

"It's done in the hills. You're playing lowland rules, and lowland rules are for people who don't know how to play."

Peyam straightened. His beard bristled. It was, Leni realized, the most animated he'd been since arriving at the shrine — more alive in this argument about card rules than he'd been about the flood, the naming, or the sleeping arrangements. He pulled the disputed card from Mava's hand, examined it, held it to the lamplight as if checking for forgery, and set it down with a firmness that suggested he was also setting down a point of principle.

"This card," he said, "is a seven."

"I know what it is."

"A seven doesn't beat a twelve."

"It does if the seven's suited and the twelve is blind."

"There's no such thing as a blind twelve."

"Then what do you call a twelve played without a bid?"

"I call it a twelve."

"And that's why you lose."

A laugh escaped from somewhere — Sem Dao, who was watching from behind Hala's shoulder. Then Rimi Mera giggled, and Bram's low chuckle followed, and suddenly the room was paying attention, and the argument between two people with a combined age of a hundred and forty-five had become the first thing at the shrine that everyone was watching because they wanted to, not because Olet told them to.

Peyam reshuffled. "We play again," he said. "Properly."

"Properly means hill rules."

"Properly means rules. One set. Agreed upon before the first card is dealt."

Mava pulled the blanket tighter around her shoulders. "Fine. But I deal."

"Why?"

"Because you shuffle like a man who learned from a book."

The laughter was real this time — wide and sudden, out before anyone could remember to be polite. Venn snorted from his corner. Deshi covered her mouth but her shoulders shook. Hala's face split into a grin that transformed her entirely — for a moment she looked like someone who wasn't keeping watch over anything, who wasn't positioned between her sister and the rest of the world. Even Bett Dao's guarded face shifted, a small upward pull at the corner of her mouth, there and gone. Jin leaned against the wall and something in his shoulders loosened — not a laugh, not quite, but the absence of the tension he carried like a second skin.

They played four more hands. Mava dealt. The rules were negotiated card by card, with arguments that kept circling back to fundamental disagreements about what constituted a legal play, and whether the hill rules were real rules or an elaborate justification for cheating, and whether Peyam's lowland rules were any better given that he was losing by three hands and his beard was doing the bristling thing again.

Gitta Tesh watched from her place near the Tesh family's mat, small and still, her hands folded in her lap. When Mava won the seventh hand with a play Peyam couldn't even identify, Gitta's mouth curved into something private and almost fond — the expression of a woman who had once played cards with people she no longer saw.

Leni found herself smiling. She was still smiling when she noticed Fen Tesh watching the game from across the hall, his dark hair in his eyes, his mouth doing the same thing hers was doing — something that wanted to be laughter but had been out of practice. Their eyes met for a half-second. They both looked away.

For ten minutes nobody thought about the flood.

Then Olet spoke.

"The news today," he said, and the leftover laughter snapped off. People straightened. Olet stood by the main hall's open side, the valley behind him invisible in the dark but audible — that steady rush of water that never stopped. The oil lamp nearest him lit the lower half of his face and left his eyes in shadow.

"The radio received a broadcast from the eastern station this afternoon. Checkpoints have been established on the main highway east of the pass. The lowland curfew has been extended from dusk to dawn. There are reports of fires and violence in the lowlands, including the capital district."

Bram leaned forward. "What kind of violence?"

"The broadcast did not specify."

"Are they sending road crews? Is anyone clearing the route?"

"There was no mention of road crews."

The silence that followed had weight. Leni could feel it pressing against the walls, against the high stone ceiling, against her own ribs. She looked at her father. Bram's jaw was tight, and his hands, which were usually in motion, had gone still on his knees. Nessa had baby Ada pressed against her shoulder, and her other hand was spread across Tomas's back, fingers splayed as if she were checking he was still there.

Venn spoke from his corner. "How long until the road is passable?"

"The water has not receded," Olet said.

"It's been four days."

"The water has not receded."

"Seasonal flooding on this road takes four to five days to drain. I've traveled this route before."

"Under normal conditions, yes. The conditions are not normal. The upstream infrastructure has been damaged. The drainage systems are not functioning."

Venn's voice hardened. "So what are you telling us?"

"I am telling you what the radio told us. The checkpoints, the curfew, the reports of violence. The water has not receded. That is what we know."

Leni looked around the room. The laughter from the card game had burned off like mist, as if it had never happened. Suna sat with Asha in her lap, one hand resting flat on the child's shin, her whole body held too still. Leni had started to recognize that stillness as the shape Suna took when she was managing a disaster she couldn't talk about. Hala had pulled Raya and Sem closer to her, a physical gathering that was also a reflex, the body's response to threat before the mind has named it. Cade Leo put his arm around Pik's shoulder. Wynn didn't move, but his hand found Cade's knee and stayed there.

Bram spoke again, and his voice was the careful voice he used when he was constructing an argument and needed every piece to land. "The political situation. The identity checks, the movement permits. That's what's keeping the road crews away. Not just the water."

"That is a reasonable interpretation," Olet said.

"So even if the water dropped tomorrow —"

"Even if the water dropped tomorrow, the road leads to a checkpoint. Whether you would be permitted to pass is not something we can know from here."

Bram sat back. He looked at Nessa. Their eyes met and held, and Leni saw something pass between her parents that she was not supposed to see. Nessa's mouth tightened. Bram gave the smallest shake of his head. It was a whole conversation anyway.

The gathering broke up slowly after that. Families retreated to their sleeping areas with the heavy quiet of people who have heard bad news and haven't figured out what to do about it. Peyam folded his cards and tucked them inside his vest with the tenderness of a man putting away something precious. Mava pulled her blanket around her shoulders and let Tila guide her back to the Kondo corner, her frail body listing slightly, the sharpness from the card game already draining from her face. The moth shadows danced on the walls as lamps were carried to sleeping quarters, each flame pulling its bubble of warm light along the stone corridors.

Leni lay on her mat in the dark. Tomas was already asleep beside her — he was ten, he could sleep through anything, his body still trusted the adults to handle whatever was happening. Ada was asleep on Nessa's chest, milk-drunk and limp. Bram and Nessa were lying down but not sleeping. Leni could hear them whispering.

She couldn't hear the words. She could hear the register — low, tight, whispering that wasn't a choice but a necessity, two people being afraid in the smallest possible voice. She caught fragments. "Checkpoints." "Papers." Her mother said something that ended on a rising sound — a question, or the word *how*, or a name Leni couldn't place. Bram whispered back. More fragments. "...morning..." and "...doesn't matter what we..." and then Nessa's hand found Bram's in the lamplight from the corridor — Leni saw it, her father's long fingers closing around her mother's smaller hand, and the grip was not comfort. It was ballast. Two people holding each other because the ground was moving and there was nothing else to hold.

Leni turned away from them. She reached into her bag and pulled out her journal and the pen she'd brought from home, from the school desk in her bedroom, a place that already felt stupidly far away. The pen was a cheap ballpoint, blue ink, almost out.

She wrote by the corridor's faint lamplight, her handwriting cramped and leaning hard to the right, the same ugly slant it got whenever she wrote in the dark or in a hurry or both. She wrote about the day. The cooking rotation and Deshi's salt commentary. The children's game that made no sense and didn't need to. Ori kissing Asha's head. Fen scraping moss off the wall with a stick. Her mother's freckles darkening in the humidity. Peyam and Mava arguing about hill rules while the flood erased the road below them and neither of them cared, because for ten minutes the game was the only real thing.

She wrote: *Nobody's coming to get us. Dad knows. Mom knows. They're just not saying it out loud because once you say it out loud you have to do something about it and there's nothing to do.*

She wrote: *Tomorrow the bell's going to ring again and we're going to get up and carry water and peel onions and the kids are going to play like nothing's wrong because nothing is wrong for them. They don't know the road goes to a checkpoint. They don't know what checkpoints mean.*

She didn't write: *I'm scared.* She didn't need to. The handwriting said it — the hard lean of the letters, the pressure of the pen against the page, the words driving hard and then cutting off.

She closed the journal and put it under her pillow and lay there listening. The flood. The rain starting up again, soft at first and then heavier, drumming on the stone overhead. Ada's small sleeping breaths. A man coughing in the Kondo section. The insects, that constant monsoon hum from the wet vegetation around the shrine, a sound so persistent you stopped hearing it until the night got quiet enough to bring it back.

There were forty-three of them on the hilltop, four days in, with no road, no phone, and nobody coming for them. Morning would come the way it did here now: people stirring before dawn, the bell sooner or later, everyone getting up to carry water, peel onions, clean, eat. By noon the courtyard would be full of kids crossing from one family to another with dirty knees and wet cuffs, like nobody had told them where the borders were supposed to be. The adults would watch and decide, depending on the minute, whether that felt like comfort or trouble.

Leni bit her thumbnail in the dark. She could taste stone dust and onion and the faint metallic residue of the pen she'd been chewing without noticing.

Day 5 would look like that. Then Day 6. Then however many days until the water went down or the checkpoints opened or someone, somewhere, figured out they were up here.

She didn't think anyone was figuring out they were up here.


# Chapter 4

By the fifth morning the shrine was waking by rote: Asha's mat damp with sweat, the cooking fire already lit somewhere beyond the partition, a hush that meant the custodians' bell had not yet rung. Suna lay still for a moment and took stock. Asha breathing against her side, the small furnace heat of a child in deep sleep. Kael in the corner of the mat, curled tight, one ankle hooked over the other. Rimi asleep with her forearm across her eyes — twelve and already perfecting the art of blocking things out. Deshi's mat empty, which meant she was up, which meant criticism of the morning meal was already being prepared alongside the rice.

Peyam was on his mat by the far wall, still asleep or pretending, his thin chest rising under the worn cotton of his shirt. His devotional beads were wound around his left wrist even in sleep. He was seventy-two and had not missed morning prayers since arriving at the shrine — up before the bell every day, crossing the courtyard to his corner of the prayer yard while the stones were still damp with the night's rain. He didn't ask Suna's permission or tell her his schedule. He simply went. Of all the Mera family members she was managing alone, Peyam was the only one who required nothing from her.

She eased Asha off her shoulder and stood. The mat needed hanging again. She rolled it, tucked it under her arm, and crossed to the courtyard. The air outside the sleeping quarters was cooler but not cold — monsoon humidity, the stones still holding the night's damp. The sound of the flood was there as always, a low massed rush from the valley below that she had stopped noticing by the second day and now only heard again when she listened for it. Like living next to a river, except the river was the road, and the road was gone. She hung the mat over the low courtyard wall and pressed it flat. The stone beneath her hands was already warming.

Breakfast was communal now, the families rotating through kitchen duty on the custodians' schedule. This morning was the Kondos' turn, and when Suna reached the cooking area under the stone overhang, Tila Kondo was already portioning rice into bowls while two of the younger Kondo cousins carried them to the main hall. The smoke from the cooking fire drifted low under the overhang, thick and sweet from monsoon-damp wood, the kind that got into clothes and hair and stayed. By the fifth day everyone at the shrine smelled of it — smoke and damp cotton and the mineral flatness of cistern water.

Deshi was there too, seated on the bench near the wall with the blue shawl she'd brought from home pulled around her shoulders, watching the Kondos work with the alert stillness of a woman conducting an inspection she hadn't been asked to perform. The shawl was frayed at both edges, pilling along the fold where she clutched it closed, and she had refused Suna's offer to wash it because it would lose what was left of its shape. It was the only thing Deshi had kept close through the whole journey — not sentiment, Suna thought, only the stubbornness of a woman who would not be separated from what was hers.

"The lentils are thin," Deshi said as Suna passed.

"They're fine."

"They're thin. More water than dal. You could read a letter through them."

"Then don't eat them."

Deshi made a sound balanced between agreement and objection — the sound of a woman who had won the exchange by provoking a response. Suna let it go and went to collect her children for breakfast.

The main hall was already filling. Forty-three people eating in shifts of twenty and twenty-three, the arrangement worked out by the custodians on the second day and adhered to since without further argument. The Mera family ate in the first shift with the Kondos and the Leos. Suna gathered Kael and Rimi — Asha was still half-asleep on Rimi's hip, her head tipped against her sister's shoulder, mouth slack — and found their spot on the long bench. The stone floor of the main hall was cool underfoot, not yet reached by the morning sun. The open side of the hall faced the valley, and the light that came through it was flat and gray, filtered through the cloud cover that hadn't fully broken in five days.

Suna had told the children their father had gone ahead. He was waiting for them at the end of the road, at their destination, and they would meet him when the water went down and the route opened again. Rimi had stopped asking about it on the third day. Kael had never asked. Asha was too young for the question. The lie turned breakfast mechanical. Her mouth kept drying out, so she had to wash every few bites down with water, and every time one of the children looked up she fixed her attention on the rice until they looked away.

Across the hall, the Kondo family occupied their usual half of the second bench, their twelve members arranged with the unconscious precision of a family that had been traveling together and knew its own geometry. Venn sat at the end, upright, eating with the deliberate pace of a man who considered meals a public act. Beside him, at the far end of the bench where the draft from the valley came up cool and steady, Mava Kondo sat with a bowl of dal in her lap, her left hand rising periodically to work at her ear, palm pressed to the cartilage in a slow looping motion as habitual and unconscious as breathing. Suna had noticed the habit on the first day and marked it the way she marked most things about other families: noted, sorted, set aside.

Asha ate slowly, picking rice grains out of her bowl with her fingers and dropping half of them on Rimi's thigh. She was four and had no interest in efficiency. Her attention moved across the hall, watching faces, watching hands, her gaze traveling from person to person with a four-year-old's undiscriminating appetite for the visible world. She touched everything — the bench, the bowl, Rimi's ear, the stone wall behind them — her hands in constant exploratory motion, absorbing textures like a second kind of sight.

She reached up and rubbed her left ear.

It was the precision that caught Suna, not the fact of it. Children touched their ears constantly — Asha especially, who touched everything. But this was not a casual scratch. Her palm settled over the cartilage and turned in slow, deliberate circles, fingers loose and slightly spread. The motion belonged to Mava. Suna looked across the hall at the old woman, still rubbing her own ear on the far bench, and then back at Asha, who had already stopped and gone to picking at her rice again, her attention elsewhere.

Suna took another bite and kept her face still. Across the hall Mava's hand made another slow circle. Asha had already gone back to her rice. Kael kicked the bench leg again, and Suna told him to stop.

The play group formed after breakfast, as it had for the past two days — the shrine's younger children sorting themselves by affinity rather than family. Kael went immediately to the Kondo boys, who had invented a game involving pebbles and a crack in the courtyard stone that seemed to function as both goal and boundary. Rimi drifted toward the older girls — Raya Dao, a few of the Kondo cousins — hovering at the edge of their circle, careful not to press in and not to stand alone either. Asha was with the younger ones: Nori Dao, Pik Leo, Ori Kondo. They had claimed the corner of the main hall near the open side where the light came in and the stone floor was warm with reflected sun, and they moved among each other with the uncomplicated physicality of children who had not yet learned to maintain the distances their parents kept.

Suna was sorting laundry — hers and Deshi's and the children's, the limited clothes they'd arrived with, washed and rewashed in the cistern until the fabric was thinning at the seams. She could see Asha from where she sat against the wall, the bright head moving among the other children, and she registered each position the way she registered everything about her children: automatically, continuously, the way you track weather or the level of water in a pot. Asha by the wall. Asha near the open side, holding Nori's hand. Asha standing next to Ori, reaching for something in Ori's pocket. Asha near Mava.

Mava was sitting in her usual spot — the section of bench near the open side where the draft came up from the valley. She had a small cloth bag in her lap, and she was sorting dried lentils with her fingers, picking out the small pale stones that hid among the grains and setting them aside in a neat row on the bench. She did this every morning after breakfast. It was her contribution to the kitchen — a task that required no standing, no lifting, no argument with Venn about whether she was overexerting herself — and she performed it with the patient sureness of someone who had been sorting lentils for longer than Suna had been alive, her fingers reading the difference between grain and stone by weight and texture alone. The stones she removed were small and smooth, washed to the same color as the lentils by decades of monsoon water, and she found them without looking.

Asha was standing near her, watching the old woman's fingers move through the grain. Mava gave her the mild automatic smile of a woman who had been around children her whole life and neither resented nor sought out their attention. Asha pointed at her and said something Suna couldn't hear across the hall.

Then she said it again, louder, turning toward Suna. "Nushi."

"What?" Suna looked up from the damp shirt in her hands.

"Nushi." Asha pointed at Mava again, then turned back to the old woman and the lentils. It was not a question. It was identification — the flat confidence of a child naming what she saw, the way she said bird or cup or Mama.

The word landed oddly. Suna repeated it once under her breath, testing its shape, and it stayed strange in her mouth. Across the hall Mava kept sorting lentils, head bent, fingers moving through grain and stone. Suna twisted water from the shirt until it ran down her wrist and went back to the laundry.

The afternoon was the dead time at the shrine. Rain started again after the midday meal — heavy, steady, the kind that lasted hours and drove everyone inside, turning the courtyard into a shallow pool of runoff that crept slowly toward the terraced garden. The main hall settled into the cramped, restless quiet of forty-three people with nowhere to go. The space was large enough for all of them, but only just — bodies shifting on benches and mats, conversations murmured in corners, the ambient hum of enforced proximity. Baby Ada Okoye was fussing. Someone was snoring. Moths had found the first lit oil lamp and were circling it in the gray afternoon light, their bodies tapping against the glass with a sound like small fingernails.

Kael was playing quietly with the pebble game he'd learned from the Kondo boys. Rimi had borrowed a book from Leni Okoye and was reading against the far wall, knees drawn up. Deshi was asleep on her mat, her blue shawl tucked under her chin, mouth open, snoring with the easy disregard of a woman who had stopped caring what she sounded like in her sleep decades ago.

Asha had worn herself out in the play group and climbed into Suna's lap after the meal, her eyelids dropping, her body performing that heavy, gradual collapse that small children did when sleep took them between one word and the next. She was asleep in minutes, her weight tipped against Suna's chest, one hand curled around a fold of Suna's shirt. Her breathing deepened and steadied.

Suna held her and watched the rain. The light in the main hall was gray and even.
