Across the hall, other families moved in their own slow orbits — Hala Dao wiping down the cooking pots with a rag, Jin Parra sitting near the wall with Melie asleep across his knees, one hand resting on her back in the absent protective gesture of a man who slept lightly. Cade Leo and his partner Wynn sat together with Pik between them, Wynn's head on Cade's shoulder, the three of them arranged in a quiet private triangle that excluded the room. Mava was on her bench, eyes closed, hands in her lap. Not rubbing. Still.

Asha shifted against Suna's chest. Her breathing stayed deep and even, the breath of real sleep, not the light doze that could be broken by a voice or a closing door. Her left hand, the one that had been gripping Suna's shirt, loosened and rose.

She brought it to her left ear and began to rub, palm laid over the cartilage, the movement slow and even.

Suna watched the hand move. The motion was precise and unhurried, the same tempo and pressure she had seen at breakfast, the same habit she had watched Mava Kondo repeat for five days. But Asha was asleep. Her eyes were closed, her body slack, her breathing unchanged. There was no one across the hall to watch, no gesture to mirror. Her hand moved against her ear with the certainty of a thing the body knew how to do on its own, without instruction or audience.

Suna sat very still. The muscles in her arms locked around Asha's warm weight. Rain hit the courtyard stones outside. The moths tapped the lamp glass. Asha's hand kept its slow circuit. Only when the hand stopped did Suna let herself move, and by then the thought was already there: I need to talk to the old woman.

---

She found Mava after the rain eased, sitting alone on the courtyard steps. The stones were wet and dark, and the sky had gone to that thick white pause between rain spells, clouds rearranging themselves before the next fall. The air was cooler and smelled of stone and wet earth and the green bite of monsoon growth pushing up faster than anything could eat it. The flood sound was louder after rain, swollen with fresh runoff, a continuous low roar from the valley that made ordinary voices feel temporary.

Mava was watching the view. From the courtyard steps you could see down the slope to where the switchback road disappeared into brown water — the point where the seven roads converged, all of them drowned. The water was the color of milked tea, opaque, slow-moving at this distance, its surface pocked with rain. A bird Suna couldn't name was working the updraft above the waterline, banking and tilting with the concentrated effort of something hunting.

Suna sat down next to her. She didn't know Mava. Five days of shared confinement had made them neighbors but not acquaintances — the proximity of shared meals and sleeping quarters and the main hall's enforced closeness, without the private conversation that would have turned proximity into familiarity. Mava was a Kondo, one of Venn's twelve, and the Kondo family kept its own close. Suna had heard Mava laugh at a card game two nights ago — a dry, low sound that carried across the hall with more force than the old woman's small frame suggested — and she had seen her sorting lentils and rubbing her ear and walking slowly to the latrine and back, and that was the full extent of what she knew about a woman who had lived seventy-three years and carried a grief Suna was about to find.

"Can I ask you something?"

Mava looked at her. Up close, her face was deeply lined, the skin around her eyes creased in a way that suggested decades of squinting against bright light — a life lived outdoors, or at altitude, or both. Her eyes themselves were sharp and dark and unhurried, the eyes of someone who had long ago stopped feeling obligated to respond quickly.

"You can ask."

"Your ear." Suna touched her own left ear. "The way you rub it. You do it all the time."

Mava's hand rose, almost involuntarily, to her ear. She caught herself and lowered it. A small embarrassed sound, almost a laugh. "Old habit. My sister used to say I'd wear a hole through it."

"Asha does it too."

The faint amusement faded. Mava's face stayed open but the quality of her attention shifted — she was listening now, not just hearing.

"My daughter. She's four. She started doing it this morning — your exact habit, left hand and all. I thought she was copying you. Kids do that. They steal gestures." Suna paused. She was aware that what she was about to say would sound unreasonable, and she could not make it sound less so, so she said it directly. "She did it in her sleep. Just now, in the main hall. She was completely asleep, and her hand came up and did it anyway."

Mava did not speak. She looked at Suna for several seconds with an expression Suna could not categorize. There was no disbelief in it, no alarm either, only the look of a person hearing a sound she had been straining to hear for a very long time.

"There's something else," Suna said. "She used a word I don't know. She pointed at you and said it. Nushi."

Mava made a sound. It was not a word. It was a small involuntary thing, the sound of air leaving the chest when the chest is struck — not hard, not violently, but in the precise place where it cannot brace. She put her hand flat on the stone step and pressed down, as if she needed to confirm that the stone was solid, that she was sitting on something that would hold.

"Say it again."

"Nushi."

Mava closed her eyes. She sat like that for several seconds, her face turned slightly toward the valley, the wet air moving past them both. When she opened her eyes they had a bright, hard shine that was not tears — the gloss of pressure held at the surface.

"My sister called me that." Her voice was low and steady, each word chosen from a very small and very precise set. "Nushi. It's from our home dialect — an old valley word from the village we grew up in, past the Anke pass. Iru used it only for me. Nobody speaks that dialect anymore. I don't think there's anyone left alive who does, except me. And I barely speak it. I just remember it."

"Your sister."

"Iru." Mava said the name the way she might set down something fragile — with both hands, with her full attention. "My younger sister. She started calling me that when we were small, and then it stuck. Even when we were grown women. Even when I told her it made me sound like a cat." The corner of her mouth lifted for a second, more memory than smile — the ghost of an old joke that had outlived the person who'd made it. "She died on this road. Forty years ago. She was coming down from the pass in a cart with a traveling group — porters, another family, I don't remember who they were — and the road gave way in the monsoon. Three people went down with it. Iru was one of them. She was twenty-nine."

She said it simply. The words had the worn smoothness of a thing told many times — not to others, Suna understood, but to herself. The private recitation of a loss that had not dimmed but had been shaped by forty years of repetition into something she could carry.

"I haven't heard anyone say that word since she died," Mava said. "It isn't a word you'd find in a book. It was just what she called me, in a language that was already dying when we were girls."

Suna did not speak for a moment. She was aware of the stone step under her, the damp seeping through the fabric of her skirt where she sat, the flood sound filling the valley below with its steady undifferentiated roar. She was aware that she had come here hoping to be told something ordinary — that "nushi" was a Kondo family nickname, a grandmother's word, something the children shared among themselves — and that she was not going to be told anything ordinary.

"Your daughter's never heard me use that word," Mava said. It was not a question. "Not here. If it comes out at all now, it comes out with Ori when she's sick or grieving, and I don't even hear myself do it."

"No," Suna said. "She hasn't."

They sat together on the wet steps. The bird was still working the updraft above the waterline. From inside the main hall came the muffled sound of children's voices — Kael's shout, the babbling run of the younger play group, a baby crying and being hushed — the ordinary noise of forty-three people crammed into a space that had been built for travelers passing through, not for travelers stuck.

Mava rubbed her ear. She caught herself doing it and this time did not stop. Her palm worked the ear in its old slow circle while she watched the valley as if the landscape had another shape underneath it.

"The road," she said. "Iru died right below us. Somewhere down there, under the water. I didn't know that when we arrived — Venn planned the route, but he never knew what stretch of road this was to me. Habel did. She was fifteen when it happened, old enough to remember the search and the way my mother sounded when they brought the body up." She paused. "I recognized the road on the second day. I've been watching the water cover the place where she fell, and I haven't said anything about it to anyone, because what would I say? It was forty years ago. The cart went off the road and she went down with it, and they found her body two days later in the river below. She was twenty-nine years old and she called me Nushi and nobody has said that word to me since."

Suna thought: twenty-nine, the same age as Jin Parra. She did not know why she thought this. It was the kind of useless, exact connection her mind produced when it had no useful response to offer — filing details into categories because the alternative was admitting that the space in front of her held no category at all.

"I don't understand how Asha could know that word," Suna said.

"No." Mava's voice was quiet. "I don't either."

Suna felt the skin tighten along her arms. If Mava had laughed, or shrugged, or named some ordinary source for the word, Suna could have filed it and gone back inside. Instead there was only the old woman beside her, the water below, and the fact itself sitting between them with nowhere to be put.

"Can I —" Suna started, and stopped. She had been about to ask if she could bring Asha to Mava, to sit them together, and she realized she did not know what she expected to happen if she did. What she expected the old woman and the child to do in each other's presence that would make this resolve into sense.

"I'd like to see her do it," Mava said quietly. "The ear. I've done that my whole life. My mother did it too. Iru did too."

"Iru did it?"

"All of us. On my mother's side. Left ear. I never noticed until Iru pointed it out — she thought it was funny. She'd do it and then point at me doing it and laugh." Mava's hand was on her ear again, and she let it stay this time. "It's just something our bodies did."

Our bodies. Not our family, not we. Our bodies — as if the gesture belonged to the flesh itself, passed along through some channel that ran beneath choice or knowledge, carried in muscle and tendon and the blind habit of a hand finding the same place on the same ear, over and over, through generations that didn't know they were repeating anything.

Suna sat with her a while longer. They did not talk much more. Mava asked after Asha — how she was sleeping, whether she was eating well, whether the damp in the sleeping quarters bothered her — the practical questions of a woman who had raised children and grandchildren and great-nieces and understood that the body was the first thing you checked, regardless of what else was happening. Suna answered. The rain started again, softly, more mist than fall, and they watched it settle on the courtyard stones and on the brown surface of the flood below.

When Suna went back inside, the main hall was dim and warm with breath and lamp-oil, the evening meal being prepared in the kitchen outside, its smoke drifting in through the open side. She found her children: Kael at his pebble game, Rimi reading, Asha on their shared mat, awake now, arranging a strip of cloth she'd found somewhere into shapes that followed a logic only she understood.

"Asha."

"Mama."

"Who's Nushi?"

Asha looked at her with the transparent certainty of a child who did not understand why an adult would ask something so obvious. She pointed through the doorway, toward the courtyard, in the direction where Mava had been sitting and might still be sitting.

"Nushi," she said.

Suna picked her up and held her, pressing her face into Asha's hair, which smelled of smoke and sweat and the warm-grain scent of a child who had been sleeping on a cotton mat all afternoon. Asha allowed it for a moment, then squirmed, and Suna let her go. She watched her daughter return to the mat and the strip of cloth, small fingers working it into a new arrangement.

That night Suna slept badly. Deshi snored on her right side, one hand tucked under her cheek. Peyam murmured through the tail end of his prayers, the beads clicking softly against each other in the dark. Rain fell and stopped and fell again, and the flood below the shrine filled the night with its low, constant sound — water over stone, water over earth, water over the place where the roads converged and drowned.

Asha slept deeply, curled against Suna's side, her face pressed into Suna's ribs. At some point in the middle of the night — the rain had paused and the only sound was the flood and the breathing of her family and the forty other lives separated from them by nothing but thin partitions of stone — Asha's left hand rose to her ear.

"Nushi," she said, not waking.

In the dark her hand found the ear again and moved there with the same patient turn.

Suna lay still and watched the hand move in the dark. The gesture no longer looked borrowed. It looked settled, at home in the body making it. By morning she would have to let somebody else see it — Mava again, maybe more than Mava. She could not keep this one inside the narrow partition of her own watching and still call it ordinary. The water below them sounded like the road giving way under Iru's feet. She held her daughter against her ribs and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 5

Venn counted before the bell. The night's new trouble had beaten him to the custodians. Twelve Kondos in the courtyard, twelve names under his breath, twelve bodies he meant to take off this hill the moment the road showed stone again. Arash sat beside him on the wall, long legs stretched out, jaw set toward the valley. There were eleven days until the wedding. Brown floodwater still ate across the switchbacks below, flattening the place where seven roads should have met cleanly. Every morning Venn made the same calculation, and every morning it came out the same: not yet.

The bell rang from the custodians' walkway. It sent a single iron note through stone.

"Everyone," Venn said.

They came at once. Tila came with the cloth wrap of needles and thread in her hand. Habel came with Ori tucked close against her hip, the girl still watching for Asha Mera whenever the families crossed paths. Mava crossed more slowly than the others, with Arash steady at her elbow. After supper the night before, Mava had told him the story in pieces, stopping twice to press her mouth flat before the sound got out. Her sister was Iru. She had been dead for forty years. She was twenty-nine when the cart went off the shrine road and dropped into the ravine. Nushi, the old valley word Iru had used only for Mava, still lived now only when Mava bent over Ori's fever or grief and forgot herself. Then Suna Mera's child had said it in her sleep. Venn had called it coincidence because the other answer would put his family inside someone else's claim.

Ger arrived behind Habel, then Rem and Osh, then the younger ones, subdued for once by the bell and the room it promised. There were twelve. All of them were here.

The naming hall was already full. Rain light washed in from the open side, gray and cold. Lamps burned along the inner wall. Bram Okoye leaned so far forward both palms were planted on his knees, ready for the room to give him an excuse. Jin Parra sat against the far wall with Melie in his lap, his face turned inward. Gitta Tesh's thumbs moved in their private circle. Hala Dao's shoulder was angled in front of Bett without seeming to be. The Leo family held themselves close together at the end of a bench. Fasa stood at the front with the valley at her back. Olet sat to one side, hands on his knees, like stone that had chosen the shape of a man.

Venn put the Kondos on the right, near the inner wall, in the arrangement that said what he needed it to say. Tila stood beside him and Arash held the place at his shoulder. Mava sat because standing too long made her knees buckle, with Habel and Ori close behind her. The others took the places they knew without asking.

Fasa began before he had fully settled.

"The naming continues," she said. "Four days ago each family spoke itself aloud. Since then this house has watched you eat, sleep, quarrel, and wake. Some things do not stay where you first set them."

Venn felt the back of his neck tighten.

"The Mera family and the Kondo family," Fasa said. "Come forward. Stand where the room can see you together."

The silence hit hard enough that even the Okoye baby stopped fussing.

Suna answered first. "No."

She held Asha against her chest and did not look at Venn. "We're not doing that. What happened between Asha and Mava stays with us. I'm not standing my daughter up for the hall."

Venn should have welcomed it. The refusal came from the other side. The Mera woman was doing his work for him.

But she had refused without even turning to him. She kept her eyes on Fasa, cutting the Kondos out of it entirely, and that slight stung worse than a direct quarrel.

"My son marries in eleven days," Venn said. He gave the words to Fasa, not to Suna. "The road is still under floodwater, and my family is waiting on it to clear. I won't have our name tied to another family's because a child made a sound in her sleep. We came here to pass through, not to be bound to strangers."

Fasa let the sentence die on the stone.

"Mava," she said.

Mava's hand rose to her left ear and began its slow circles. Ori saw it and caught Habel's wrist with both hands.

Venn shook his head once.

Fasa did not look at him. "Speak for yourself, Mava Kondo. Tell the room what Suna Mera asked you yesterday."

Mava lowered her hand to her lap. "The child used a word."

"Say it."

Mava swallowed. "Nushi."

The word sat in the middle of the hall. Venn heard rain against the open stone, the soft gutter of lamp oil, somebody dragging a breath through a narrow throat.

"Whose word is it?" Fasa asked.

"My sister's." Mava's voice was thin but steady. "Iru's. Old valley speech from Ishe, where she married. It means something like the other half of my face. No one here speaks it. No one here should know it. Iru has been dead forty years."

Fasa turned to Suna. "Do you know the valley speech of Ishe?"

"No."

"Has anyone in your family traveled there?"

"No."

Fasa looked past Suna. "Deshi. Peyam."

Old Peyam opened his eyes, beads cupped in both hands, and gave one deliberate shake of his head.

Deshi said nothing. She fixed on Mava so completely the rest of the hall might as well have emptied.

"Then say what is in front of you," Fasa said. "A child has spoken a dead woman's word with no road to it that anyone in this room can name. Stand together and answer to that."

Suna's arms tightened around Asha. "I already answered."

Fasa kept her eyes on her. "I heard the refusal. The thing itself is still here."

Venn rose before he knew he had decided to.

"This is grief," he said. The hall gave his voice back to him. "My aunt's grief. A word she's carried in her mouth for forty years. A four-year-old saying something strange does not make a bond between families. It does not rewrite who we are. I won't have my family hauled into a story because a ritual wants more than the facts can bear."

No one answered him. Bram leaned farther forward. Jin stared at the floor between his feet. Cade Leo put a hand over Pik's shoulder and left it there. Even the children were quiet.

Then Deshi stood.

Venn had filed her days ago: the sharp old woman from the Mera side, measuring rice by bite and children by noise. He knew the type because his own family had made women like that and depended on them. She should have stayed behind Suna, hard and traditional and on her own side of the room.

She crossed the hall instead.

Her sandals made almost no sound. She stopped in front of Mava's bench and looked down at her.

"Your sister," Deshi said. "What was her name?"

"Iru."

"And Nushi was hers for you?"

"Yes."

Deshi turned her head toward Asha for one beat, then back to Mava. "My granddaughter didn't get that from me."

She sat beside Mava on the Kondo bench.

Suna made a small shocked sound. "Deshi."

"Be quiet," Deshi said.

She took Mava's hands in both of hers and settled around them with an old, practiced certainty.

"Tell me about Iru."

Mava looked down at the hands around hers. Her throat worked once, twice. Then the sound came out of her ragged and low, and Ori buried her face in Habel's skirt.

"She was four years younger," Mava said. "Pretty. Spoiled. Always laughing at me for worrying. She married into Ishe and kept talking about the valley as if she'd invented it. She was coming home to see me when the cart went over right below the shrine turn. They brought her back on a door because the wheels had shattered."

Deshi did not release her hands.

"Twenty-nine," Mava said. "That's how old she was. Twenty-nine."

Venn stayed on his feet because sitting would have looked like surrender and stepping forward would have put his hands on a woman who did not belong to him. Tila's fingers touched the inside of his wrist, light as thread tension.

***

Fasa did not call the room back to order. She stayed where she was and let the silence stretch around the bench where the two old women sat. No one rose. No one looked to Olet. The hall had tilted toward Mava's open mouth and would not tilt back.

She wept without trying to stop. She did not weep loudly. The tears kept dropping off her jaw onto the front of her blouse while Deshi sat close and held on. Habel moved to Mava's other side and pressed a palm between her shoulder blades. Arash stayed behind the bench with his hands in his pockets and did not once glance at Venn. The arrangement Venn had built at the start of the hour came apart around them and stayed apart.

He sat down because his knees did it first.

Across the hall Suna spoke into the quiet. "She said it asleep. I'd never heard it from her awake. She was dreaming, and her hand was on her ear, and she said Nushi like she knew who she was calling."

Nessa pulled the baby tighter. Bram's mouth stayed open a moment before he shut it. Venn felt damp air on the sweat cooling under his shirt.

Gitta's thumbs were still circling each other. Fen Tesh watched the bench without blinking, shoulders drawn tight enough that even his next breath seemed delayed.

Bram cleared his throat. "Then let's be exact about what we're saying. Either the child made a sound that happened to resemble a word, or we're being asked to admit that something passed into her that no one can account for. Those are not the same claim."

"Belief is your own work," Fasa said. "This room is for naming what it has seen."

"What I've seen is an old woman crying and a child touching her ear."

Nessa's hand landed on Bram's forearm. He nodded once and shut his mouth on the rest, corrected by the touch before the room could do it for him.

"I'm not dismissing it," he said. "I'm asking what you expect us to do with it."

"Not turn away," Fasa said.

Venn stood again.

"Turn away from what?" he said. "From my aunt grieving her sister? From a child not knowing what comes out of her own mouth? Iru is ours. Her death is ours. The road took her from our family, not from this room."

Deshi lifted her face to him. Her eyes were dry and steady.

"A dead woman's word is in my granddaughter's mouth," she said. "What should I do with that? Pretend I did not hear it? Pretend your aunt is not crying into my hands because no one living has said that word to her in forty years?" She looked at him the way she might have looked at a pot she knew had been left too long on the fire. "You are the man who likes order. Tell me the orderly response."

He had answers for dowries, property lines, sleeping assignments, routes, debts, and the right shape of a wedding visit. He opened his mouth on that habit and found nothing there.

"The Kondo family will speak on this privately," he said at last.

Arash looked away. Bram shifted but did not pick up the offer. Fasa remained still.

"Private speech made this room necessary," she said. "What hides in families comes here to be spoken where others can hear it."

Tila broke in before he could answer. "Enough. He's heard it. Mava's shaking. Let the old woman breathe."

Fasa's gaze moved to Olet. He sat as he had sat all morning, hands on knees, unreadable. After a moment she nodded.

"We stop here for today," she said.

Still no one scattered. Peyam rose from the Mera side with his beads looped over one wrist and crossed the hall to the bench. Habel made room. He sat beside Mava without a word, another old body bracing the space around her. Bett's eyes never left Mava. Hala leaned in and whispered something to her sister that Venn could not catch. Jin was the last of the untouched families to go; he paused in the doorway with Melie on his hip and looked back once before the rain took him out of sight.

When the hall had thinned, Mava spoke again.

"She was coming to visit me," she said. Her voice was rubbed raw. "Iru had jars of quince preserve wrapped in cloth under the seat because she knew I liked it and never made it right. She rubbed my ear when I was a child and when I was old enough to be ashamed of crying. She'd say Nushi and laugh at my face and I would stop. I still do it because of her." She pressed her fingertips to her ear and let them fall. "I just stopped doing it where people could see."

Deshi nodded once. "Then the word did not stay buried with her."

No one contradicted her. Habel kept her hand on Mava's back. Peyam bowed his head over his beads. Even Fasa said nothing.

Tila touched Venn's sleeve. "Come outside."

They stood under the kitchen overhang with rain dripping from the edge of the roof hard enough to splash their sandals. The banked fire smelled of wet ash. Beyond the courtyard wall the flood kept up its blunt roar.

For a while Tila said nothing. She let the rain fill the space and waited, knowing a question too soon would only lock him harder.

"The wedding is in eleven days," Venn said.

"I know."

"The road is still gone."

"I know."

"And now this word."

The sentence stopped there. He had nothing that fit after it.

Inside the hall Mava's voice carried out in scraps, low and steady now, telling Deshi things he had never heard her tell him. He could feel the story leaving the hall already.

Tila looked toward the doorway. "Mava hasn't cried for Iru since the funeral."

Rain struck stone in quick hard beats. Venn could still see Deshi taking the floor from everyone else, Arash standing behind the bench and not coming to his side, the whole room holding still when he tried to drag it back under him.

"She's crying now," he said.

"Because somebody finally asked for the whole thing," Tila said. "Not the custodian. Deshi."

He did not answer. Under the overhang he counted without looking, the old habit moving in him on its own: Tila at his side, Mava inside, Habel and Ori with her, Ger, Arash, Rem, Osh, the younger ones in the sleeping room. There were twelve Kondos under one roof.

The count landed and did nothing.


# Chapter 6

The next afternoon, the children had taken over the lower terrace.

Jin watched from the kitchen overhang where he'd been scrubbing pots for the past hour — volunteer work, the kind nobody argued about, because it kept him useful and silent and away from the naming hall where the adults were still sorting through whatever had happened yesterday between Mava Kondo and Suna Mera's mother-in-law. He didn't want to know. He didn't want to have an opinion about it. He wanted to scrub the black crust off the rice pot and watch his daughter.

Melie was in the middle of the group, which was new. For the first four days she'd circled the edges, watching the Kondo children and the Mera boy with the cautious hunger Jin recognized because he'd felt it at her age — wanting to be included but not knowing how to step in. She'd stood near Rimi Mera once, close enough that the twelve-year-old had to notice her, and Rimi had handed her a smooth pebble without looking down, an offhand kindness that Melie had carried in her pocket for two days afterward, turning it over and over until the warmth of her hand was indistinguishable from the warmth of the stone.

Now she sat cross-legged next to Nori Dao, the two of them arranging sticks in a pattern that seemed to involve rules Jin couldn't follow. Nori was a year older and a head taller and kept rearranging Melie's sticks without asking, and Melie — his Melie, who had spent three months going quiet at the table, who had stopped singing in the bath, who had developed the habit of checking the front door whenever she heard footsteps — didn't object. She just waited for Nori to finish correcting her placement and then added her next stick with careful concentration. The patience was new. Or not new. Recovered. She'd been a patient child before Reva left.

Jin's hands stopped in the water. He made himself keep scrubbing. The pot was copper, dented at the rim, and the burnt rice came away in black flakes that dissolved between his fingers. He pressed his thumb against the metal hard enough to whiten the nail. The water was cold from the cistern, cold enough that his knuckles ached, and he pressed harder.

Reva had walked out on a Tuesday. He remembered the day because she'd left her lunch in the fridge — the container she packed every morning, the one with the cracked blue lid — and he'd come home from the site and seen it sitting there on the shelf and known before he checked the closet, before he found the note, before he counted what was missing. She'd left her lunch. She hadn't planned on going that morning. Or she had planned it and then still packed her lunch out of habit and then left it there and walked out anyway, and he didn't know which version was worse — the impulsive abandonment or the deliberate one that was so rehearsed she could go through the motions of staying even while she was leaving.

He'd told Melie that her mother had died. He'd told her that evening, quickly, before anyone else could tell her something different, because Reva's sister knew and her sister's husband knew and Jin could already see the machinery of explanation gearing up around him, the concerned faces and the careful words and the particular head-tilt of pity that precedes unsolicited advice. He'd needed to control the story. A dead mother was clean. A dead mother couldn't come back. A dead mother wasn't a woman who looked at her daughter and her husband and their kitchen with the cracked blue container still in the fridge and decided she wanted something else.

The shame of it lived in where other people's eyes went first: the sink, the unmade bed, Melie's shoes by the door. Men whose wives died got casseroles, soft voices, neighbors who checked in and brought extra food on weekends. Men whose wives left got the quick sideways look, the change of subject, the pause before sympathy turned into appraisal. He'd watched it happen in the first week after Reva walked out, before he'd settled on the lie, when his brother-in-law stood in the kitchen doorway and let his gaze move over the room before it landed on Jin. Maybe they were right to look. A woman had looked at this life and gone.

He pulled the pot from the basin and set it upside down on the stone ledge to dry. His hands were red and his knuckles were swelling slightly from the cold. Below him, on the terrace, Melie laughed.

The sound went through him like a needle. She was laughing at something Nori had done — knocked over the entire stick arrangement, maybe, or said something in the blunt way that Nori had, a seven-year-old's declarative certainty about the world that seemed to delight Melie in a way that adult gentleness never did. Jin watched his daughter's face: open, unguarded, the gap where her front teeth used to be visible because she was laughing with her whole mouth. She hadn't laughed like that since before Reva left. Since before the note, the empty closet, the lie.

She was thriving here. In this stone compound surrounded by strangers who asked too many questions and custodians who rang a bell for every meal and prayer and an old woman who wanted to know the maiden name of his dead wife. She was thriving because there were other children and because no one here knew the specific shape of what was missing from her life, and the story Jin had told at the naming — my wife passed, we're traveling to her family — sat over the truth like a lid, and the lid let Melie move through this temporary world without the particular cruelty of being the girl whose mother chose to leave.

She was the girl whose mother chose to leave. He couldn't even think the words without his mouth bracing for the catch before the consonant. He pressed his teeth together and waited for it to pass. It always passed. It had been doing this since he was twelve, long before Reva, long before any of this, and it always passed.

He stacked the clean pots on the ledge, dried his hands on the cloth hanging from the overhang post, and stood watching his daughter play until the light shifted and the shadows of the compound walls reached the lower terrace and the children began to scatter toward their families.

---

Bett Dao was at the cistern when Jin brought Melie down to wash her hair before the evening meal.

She was filling a clay jug, one hand braced on the stone basin's lip, and she straightened when she heard them on the steps. Her sleeves were rolled to her elbows in precise folds, the kind of careful cuffing Jin associated with people who'd worked with their hands for years — tidiness as reflex, not vanity. She wore a thin brass ring on her right hand, tarnished nearly black, that she turned with her thumb while she waited for the jug to fill. She nodded at him — the same flat acknowledgment she gave everyone, not unfriendly but not an opening — and went back to her water.

"Papa, it's cold," Melie said, touching the surface with one finger and pulling it back.

"I know. It's always cold." He'd washed her hair here twice since they'd arrived, both times in the late afternoon when the stone basin caught the last warmth from the sun. The water came from a spring somewhere beneath the shrine, cold even in monsoon heat, and Melie complained about it every time with the specific outrage of a child who has been warned about something unpleasant and then discovers that the warning did not make it less unpleasant.

"Nori says her aunt washes her hair with soap flowers," Melie said, looking at Bett's back. "She says they make your hair smell like the garden."

"We don't have soap flowers."

"Maybe Bett has some."

"We're not going to bother her. Come here."

Jin cupped water in both hands and brought it to Melie's head. She ducked and squealed and he caught her by the shoulder, firm but careful, the grip of a parent whose child was always about to dart away from the one thing that needed doing. "Hold still. I'll be fast."

The water ran through her dark hair and down the back of her neck. She shivered and he felt the shiver travel up through his hand into his wrist. Her hair was getting long — past her shoulders now, the longest it had ever been. Reva had always kept it trimmed, sitting Melie on a kitchen stool with a towel around her shoulders and cutting with the good scissors, the ones with the orange handles that were still in the drawer at home. Jin didn't know how to cut hair evenly. Melie didn't seem to mind the length, or if she minded she hadn't said, and he hadn't asked because asking might surface the question of why her mother wasn't here to do it.

He worked the soap through her hair — a brown block the custodians provided, harsh and unscented, the kind that stripped everything — his thumbs moving along her scalp in the pattern he'd learned from watching Reva do this. His hands remembered the small circles. The gentleness required at the temples where the skin was thin. His hands had learned this from a woman who was alive and somewhere else, living a life that didn't include the small warm skull under his fingers, and his hands did it anyway because muscle memory doesn't care about betrayal.

"Papa."

"Hmm."

"Is Nori's mum here?"

His fingers went still in her soapy hair. "I'm not sure about their family," he said. He didn't know the Daos' arrangement beyond the obvious — Bett and her older sister Hala, Sem and Raya usually with Hala, Nori orbiting Bett so naturally that Jin had stopped trying to sort out how the household had been arranged before they all got stranded here. He hadn't asked, because asking invited questions in return, and questions were the one thing he couldn't afford.

Bett was still at the basin, her jug full now, but she hadn't moved to leave. She was listening — not obviously, her body angled away, her free hand worrying a thread loose from her sleeve cuff — but listening in the way Jin recognized because he did it himself: face turned from the conversation, attention fixed on every word.

"I am," Bett said, without turning around. Her voice was even, unperformative. "Nori's mine. Hala's my sister."

"Oh." Melie's face was upside down, looking up at Jin through the fall of her wet hair. "Then Hala's the aunt with the soap flowers?"

"Yeah," Bett said. "That's Hala."

Jin reached for the tin cup by the basin and rinsed Melie's hair, pouring with one hand and shielding her eyes with the other, and Melie endured it with the stoic misery of a child who has learned that protest doesn't shorten the process.

Bett had turned and was watching them. Not watching — something less intrusive. She held the jug on her hip, elbow locked beneath the weight, and her face, which Jin had only ever seen from across the hall as a guarded surface, was doing something he hadn't expected. She was looking at Melie's wet hair and Jin's careful hands with an expression that wasn't pity and wasn't judgment. It looked like memory. Like she was watching something she had done once, for someone, in another kitchen, and the watching cost her something she was choosing to pay.

Then she turned and climbed the steps with the jug on her hip, and the moment closed behind her.

Jin wrung Melie's hair between his palms and twisted it into the rough shape of a braid. He didn't know how to braid properly. Another thing Reva had done. Another skill he'd never learned because he'd assumed she would always be there to do it, and the assumption had felt so solid, so much like ground, that he'd never noticed he was standing on something that could move.

"Come on," he said. "Dinner soon."

Melie took his hand. Her fingers were cold and small and damp, and she swung his arm as they climbed the steps, and Jin held on.

---

The evening meal was set out on the stone ledge that served as the communal table — flatbreads stacked under a cloth, a lentil pot still bubbling over the last of the coals, rice with fried shallots, and three small bowls of condiments the Dao women had prepared from their own stores. Jin knew the Daos had been on kitchen rotation because Hala had been there all afternoon, her broad back bent over the cutting board, humming something under her breath while she worked, filling the kitchen with sound the way other people filled it with talk. Bett had been grinding spices in the stone mortar when Jin had passed through with Melie on the way to the cistern, her wrist turning in steady circles while the stone gave back a wet rasp under each pass, her face absent and focused at the same time, as if the mortar were holding a thought steady for her.

The children ate together, as they had since the third or fourth day — a cluster of bodies on the lower step, plates balanced on knees, the older ones helping the younger ones without being told. Rimi Mera tore flatbread for Asha, who was sitting in Rimi's lap, four years old and unbothered by the fact that she was using a stranger's twelve-year-old as furniture. Kael held his own plate with the defensive grip of an eight-year-old who has been told he is old enough to manage. The Kondo children ate in their own tight group — Venn's influence, visible even at mealtimes in the way they kept their elbows close and their voices down — but Ori, the youngest at nine, kept glancing over at the other children, then back to her own plate, then over again, as if the space between the groups might bite.

Melie sat between Nori and Sem Dao. Sem was eleven, quiet, with the same compact build as his aunt Bett and the same economy of movement — he did things once and correctly, without wasted effort, as though he'd been taught that resources were finite and motion was a resource. He'd started saving a spot for Melie at meals without being asked, a small territorial gesture that Melie accepted without comment, the way children accept kindness they haven't been taught to question.

Jin ate standing, leaning against the kitchen overhang's wall, tearing bread and scooping lentils without tasting either. He'd taken some of the fried shallot rice and a smear of the brown-gold paste from one of the small bowls — rich, sweet, with a depth he vaguely identified as nut-based. He ate mechanically. The food was good. The Daos could cook. He chewed and swallowed and watched his daughter.

Melie was eating with both hands on her bread, tearing and dipping with the focused intensity of a child who takes food seriously. She dipped her torn bread into the brown-gold paste, took a large bite, chewed, dipped again. Nori was telling her something about a game they would play tomorrow, and Melie nodded while she chewed, her attention split between the conversation and the food with the particular greed of a six-year-old who hasn't fully learned to pretend she isn't hungry.

She paused. Her hand stopped halfway to her mouth. She looked at the bread in her fingers as if she'd never seen bread before.

"Melie?" Jin set his plate on the ledge.

She scratched her neck. Then her forearm. Both movements quick and distracted, the way you scratch at a mosquito bite without deciding to. She took another bite. She chewed. She scratched her neck again, harder.

"What's wrong?" He was already moving toward her, not running, but the five meters between the kitchen wall and the lower step felt like a distance he couldn't cross fast enough, each stride landing on stone that seemed to stretch.

"Itchy," Melie said. She scratched her forearm and Jin saw the skin — raised, blotchy, a red rash spreading from her wrist to the inside of her elbow in the time it took him to reach her.

He knelt beside her. "Show me."

She held up both arms. The rash was on both now, blooming outward from the insides of her elbows, welts rising under the skin like something pushing up from beneath. Her face was changing — the skin around her mouth puffing, her lower lip thickening, a flush climbing her cheeks that had nothing to do with the evening heat. She scratched at her throat with both hands.

"I feel funny," she said. Her voice was wrong. Thicker, squeezed, a six-year-old's voice forced through a narrowing passage.

Jin grabbed her plate. Bread, lentils, the brown-gold paste smeared across the edge. He looked at what she'd eaten and didn't know what was wrong and the not-knowing opened a hole in his chest that felt exactly like falling because he was her father and he was supposed to know. "What did you eat? Melie, what did you eat just now?"

"The bread. And the—" She coughed. A small cough, nothing, except beneath it was a wheeze Jin had never heard from her, a high thin scraping sound like air pulled through wet cloth. She coughed again. Her eyes were watering.

"Melie." He pulled her into his arms. She was light — she'd always been small for six, the kind of lightness that made strangers guess she was younger — and her body was hot against his chest, hotter than the evening warranted, and the rash was spreading down her neck now, red welts rising on skin he'd been washing with his own hands twenty minutes ago. "Breathe. Just breathe for me."

She tried. The wheeze was louder. Her fingers went to her throat, scratching, pulling at the skin as if she could widen the passage by hand.

"Something's wrong with her." He said it to the courtyard, to everyone, to anyone. Adults were turning. Suna had already stood from where she'd been eating with Deshi and the children. Hala was coming out of the kitchen, wiping her hands on her apron, her face shifting from confusion to something faster than confusion. "Something's wrong — she ate something, I don't know what—"

"Let me see." Bett was beside him. She'd been sitting four meters away with Nori, eating from her own plate, and now she was here and he hadn't seen her cross the space. Her hand went to Melie's face — not gently, efficiently. Chin up. Lips checked. Two fingers under the angle of Melie's jaw where the swelling was worst.

"Her throat's closing," Bett said. Her voice dropped, and the words still cut clean through the courtyard. "What did she eat?"

"Bread, lentils, the—"

Bett picked up Melie's plate. She touched the smear of paste with her finger and brought it to her nose. "Cashew," she said.

"She's eaten cashews before. She eats them all the time, she's never—"

"Not this." Bett set the plate down. She was moving fast now, her body stripped of everything that wasn't functional, her hands already reaching behind her as if she expected something to be placed in them. She looked at Melie's swelling face and something in her locked into place — not coldness, the opposite. "Hala. The kit. Go."

Hala ran. Not walked, not hurried — ran, her feet slapping the stone, back into the kitchen area where the Daos kept their things, and the sound of containers being shoved aside carried across the courtyard.

"What's happening to her?" Jin's voice broke on the second word. Melie was wheezing against his chest, each breath a whistle through a gap that was narrowing by the second, and her fingers were clawing at her throat hard enough to leave red marks on skin that was already welted, and her eyes were swollen to slits and he could feel her ribs heaving against his forearms, each inhalation pulling her ribcage wide as her body fought for air that wasn't coming fast enough.

"Anaphylaxis." Bett said the word like it had been worn smooth from use. "Tree-nut allergy. Cashew. It's our family's allergy. Sem has it. My son had it."

The words didn't arrange themselves into sense. His daughter was choking in his arms and this woman he'd spoken to three times in a week was saying something about families and allergies and a son, and Melie was making a sound now — a high, thin, aspirated whistle on each inhale, air forced through a space the width of a straw — and the sound was the most terrifying thing Jin had ever heard because it was the sound of something that could stop.

"Hold her still." Bett took Melie from his arms. She didn't ask. One arm under Melie's back, the other supporting her head to keep the airway as straight as possible, she lifted her in a single clean motion and set her on the stone step, propped against her own bent knee. "Melie, I know it's scary. Don't scratch. Look at me."

Melie looked at her. Her eyes were wet and swollen and terrified, her face a stranger's face, puffed and distorted, and her hands were still going for her throat. Bett caught both small wrists in one hand and held them against Melie's chest. "You're going to breathe. It's tight but you're going to breathe. Look at me."

Hala came back with a tin box — battered, scratched, a dent in the lid that had been hammered mostly flat. Bett opened it one-handed, her other hand still holding Melie's wrists. Inside: a small glass vial and a syringe wrapped in white cloth. The cloth was clean and carefully ironed, the creases sharp, and this was the detail that would stay with Jin afterward — the ironed cloth, the exact folds, the way the battered tin had been kept as if order itself could hold a throat open.

Bett drew the dose with the same economy she brought to everything else. She glanced once at the vial and filled the syringe without pausing. The dosage seemed to live in her fingers, not in her head.

"Epinephrine," she said to Jin. She didn't look at him. Her eyes were on Melie's face, reading the swelling, measuring the progression with an attention that was clinical and also something more than clinical — something personal and hard-earned. "It'll open her airway. Her heart's going to race. It'll scare her but it's the drug working."

"Do it." He didn't know what epinephrine was. He didn't care what it was. Melie was wheezing and her lips were going grey and her fingernails were digging into Bett's palm where Bett held her wrists and Jin would have let this woman pour fire into his daughter's body if it meant the whistling would stop.

Bett pushed Melie's shirt up and exposed her outer thigh — the skin there still clear of rash, pale and goosebumped in the cooling air. She drove the needle in. No flinch, no tentative pause. She pressed the plunger with the steady force of practice, and then she held the needle in place and counted — silently, her lips moving, her thumb keeping the muscle steady while the drug entered.

She withdrew the needle and set the syringe on the stone. "Now we wait."

The courtyard was quiet. Forty-odd people, some standing and some still sitting with plates in their laps, watching. Suna had her hand on Asha's head, holding her daughter against her hip. Hala stood behind Bett with her arms crossed over her chest, her face tight and furious with worry. The Kondo children had stopped eating. Old Peyam had put down his pipe. Nori Dao was standing exactly where Melie had been sitting a minute ago, holding the plate Melie had been eating from, her face white and absolutely still. She knew what this was. She had grown up knowing.

Melie breathed. The whistle was still there. Bett's palm was flat between Melie's shoulder blades, feeling each breath — checking the vibration, the depth, the resistance, the way you'd feel a pipe for blockage, your hand reading what your ears couldn't measure. Twenty seconds. Thirty. Jin counted with her, or tried to, but the numbers kept slipping because his hands were shaking and his jaw was locked and the sound of that whistle was still there, still narrowing, still the width of a straw—

And then it changed. Loosened. Deepened. The sound of a passage widening by millimeters, the wheeze dropping half a tone, and Bett's hand went still on Melie's back.

"There," Bett said.

Melie took a breath that sounded like breathing. Then another. The swelling around her eyes was still bad — her face still looked distorted, unfamiliar — but the color was coming back into her lips and her breathing was slowing from panicked shallow gasps to something Jin's body recognized before his mind did: she was getting air. She was going to keep getting air.

"Papa," Melie said. Her voice was hoarse and her eyes were still nearly shut. "My heart's going really fast."

"I know. That's the medicine. It's supposed to do that." He was repeating what Bett had told him. He was reaching for Melie, pulling her from Bett's knee into his own arms, and she came — hot and shaking and alive, her heart hammering against his chest through both their shirts, her breathing ragged but open, open, the air going in and coming out. He pressed his face into her damp hair and smelled the harsh soap from the cistern and the faint residual sweetness of the cashew paste and his own sweat, and he held her, and his hands were shaking so badly that he could feel them vibrating against her back like something mechanical.

"She can't eat tree nuts," Bett said. She was still sitting on the step, her hands on her thighs, the syringe on the stone beside her. The ironed cloth had come open and the glass vial caught the last daylight, a small bright point. Her voice was steady but her jaw was set in a way that Jin recognized as effort — steadiness as a choice, not a condition. "Cashews, pistachios, walnuts. Almonds are sometimes safe but I wouldn't test it here. Not without a clinic."

"She's never—" Jin's jaw locked. The consonant caught and held, the old hesitation, and for a moment he couldn't make the word come out. He pressed his mouth against Melie's hair and waited for his throat to release. "She's never been allergic. To anything. She eats everything."

"She does now," Bett said.

Its flatness startled him. It wasn't cruel. It wasn't bewildered. It wasn't wrapped in any of the language the compound had been generating for a week about manifestations and connections and what the naming revealed about families. It was just the fact, set down on the stone beside the syringe and the vial and the ironed cloth: your daughter has this thing now. It is real. It will not go away because it shouldn't be there.

Hala knelt beside Bett and put a hand on her sister's shoulder. Bett didn't lean into the touch. She didn't acknowledge it except by not pulling away. She sat on the stone step with Hala's hand on her shoulder and looked at Melie breathing in Jin's arms, and Jin saw her gaze go to the spot on Melie's thigh where the needle had entered — a small red dot, already closing, unremarkable. Whatever Bett saw in that dot lived in a place Jin didn't have access to. He understood, without knowing the details, that she had done this before for someone who was not here anymore.

"I've got one more dose," Bett said. She nodded at the tin. "After that we'd need to get to a clinic, and the road's not open."

"What if it happens again?"

"Don't let her eat tree nuts."

"I don't know what has tree nuts in it." His voice was rising and he couldn't stop it — the flood, the loss of volume control, the specific humiliation of a man unable to protect his child from a bowl of paste at dinner. "I've never had to know. She ate cashews at home, I gave her cashews, I put them in her — she's eaten them her whole—"

"So come to me before she eats." Bett stood. She brushed the stone dust from her knees — one brisk pass of each hand, no fuss. The syringe went back into the tin, the vial beside it, the cloth folded over both with the same careful creases. She closed the lid and tucked the tin under her arm. "I've been managing this allergy for eleven years. Sem's had it since he was born. I can tell you what's safe and what's not."

Jin stared at her. He was holding Melie against his chest and his daughter's heart was still hammering and this woman was offering to manage what he couldn't manage, to know what he didn't know, and the offer was made without softness, without the gentle tone people used when they felt sorry for you. It was made the way you'd hand someone a tool: here, you need this, take it.

"Why does she have your family's allergy?" He didn't mean it as accusation. He meant it as the question it was — the thing he could not hold inside his body, because his daughter had just had her throat close from a condition she didn't have yesterday, a condition that belonged to strangers, and a woman he barely knew had saved her with a needle and a counted silence and hands that knew exactly where to push the plunger.

Bett looked at him. The guarded surface was back — the face she showed the compound, economical, closed. But the muscles around her eyes were tight in a way that had nothing to do with composure and everything to do with something she was choosing not to say.

"I don't know why," she said. "I just know what it looks like."

She climbed the steps. Hala followed, her hand briefly touching the back of Bett's arm as they went — a sisterly gesture, practiced and unremarkable, the habit of two women who had been looking after each other for a long time. Nori was still holding Melie's plate. Bett took it from her daughter's hands as she passed — gently, the way you take something with an edge from a child — and set it face-down on the ledge where no one else would eat from it.

The courtyard emptied in stages. The crisis was already becoming the thing people would talk about in low voices after the children slept — did you see it, how fast, the Dao woman's kit, how did she know. Suna caught Jin's eye as she passed with Asha on her hip and Rimi trailing behind. She didn't speak. Her gaze moved from Melie to the food ledge to Asha's face and back again, already recalculating — her children, the food, what else might change without warning, whether the world she thought she understood had shifted again beneath all of them.

Jin sat on the step with Melie in his arms. Her heart was still going too fast. He could feel it through her ribs, a hummingbird tremor against his chest, and he kept hearing Bett's voice — it's the drug working, it'll scare her but it's working — and repeating it to himself as if the repetition could reach whatever part of him was still falling through the hole that had opened when he heard that first wheeze. Her breathing was steady now. Regular. The swelling was going down, slowly, the puffiness around her eyes receding enough that she looked like herself again — the gap teeth, the dark hair he'd just washed, the chin that was Reva's chin. He saw it every time. Every single time.

"Papa," she said. Her voice was scratchy and small. "My throat still feels weird."

"It'll get better. The medicine's still working."

"How did she know what to do?"

"Because someone in her family has the same thing you have."

"But I don't have it. I ate cashews at Auntie's house. I ate them loads of times."

"I know."

"So why did my throat do that?"

He didn't have an answer. He had a lie about her dead mother and the copper pot he'd scrubbed that afternoon and the memory of her laughing on the terrace twenty minutes before her throat closed, and he had the present fact of her body in his arms — hot, trembling, breathing — and a debt he owed to a woman who'd known what was happening because her family carried the same thing, her child, her nephew, her blood, and now his daughter's blood too, impossibly, undeniably, a connection written in the body that no story he could construct would explain away.

"I don't know," he said.

Melie pressed her face into his shirt and didn't ask again.

The custodians' bell rang for evening prayers. The compound moved around them. Somewhere above, Bett's voice carried briefly — saying something to Nori, ordinary words, a mother's evening instructions — and then the compound settled into its nightly routine of mats and lamps and the sound of rain beginning again on stone.

Later, when Jin carried Melie to their sleeping space and laid her down and sat beside her with his back against the wall, he found the tin box on the floor next to their mat. Bett had left it without telling him. No note, no instructions, no explanation. Just the dented tin with the ironed cloth inside and the single remaining dose of the drug that would keep his daughter breathing if this new, impossible thing in her body decided to close her throat again.

He sat with the tin in his hands and listened to Melie breathe and understood something with the particular clarity that comes only when terror has passed and left the ground rearranged. He could not keep her safe alone. He had never been able to keep her safe alone. The lie he'd built — the dead wife, the self-sufficient father, the man who needed nothing from anyone — was exactly as strong as Melie's certainty that she could eat cashews. Both had been true this morning. Neither was true tonight.

Around him the compound breathed. Rain on stone, mats shifting, a baby crying somewhere — Ada, the Okoye infant, her small voice carrying through the thin walls and then stopping as someone picked her up. The flood's steady rush from the valley below, unchanged, indifferent. Melie's breathing beside him, even and open.

The old catch fired on nothing — no word, no thought he was trying to form, just the body's habit of seizing before speech. He pressed his thumbs against the dented lid of the tin and held it in his lap and listened to the rain keep hitting the courtyard stone and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 7

The naming bell was not beautiful. Leni had settled this on the third day and nothing since had changed her mind. It was a flat, bossy sound — bronze on bronze with no sustain to it, no ring, just the blunt announcement that someone wanted your attention and didn't care whether you wanted to give it. It reminded her of the substitute teachers at her old school, the ones who rapped the desk with a ruler because they'd already lost the room and had nothing else.

Day nine, and the courtyard stones still held the night's rain. She knew because she'd been counting in her journal, a line drawn under each date at the bottom of the page, and because Tomas had asked that morning. He'd wanted the exact number. She'd said nine and he'd said *that's a long time* with the flat certainty of a ten-year-old stating a fact, and she'd said *yeah* and he'd gone back to throwing stones at the courtyard wall.

She unfolded herself from the steps and went inside.

The main hall had developed a seating geography nobody had assigned but everybody maintained. The Kondos filled the right side — twelve of them, impossible to miss, a family that seemed to travel by census count. Venn sat at the end nearest the open wall where you could see the valley and the brown water below, a position that let him look outward when the naming bored him and inward when it threatened him. Tila sat beside him, her hands in her lap, her posture a careful complement to his — not subservient but orbiting, the practiced geometry of three decades with a man who took up a great deal of space. The Meras anchored the left, near the kitchen passage. Deshi sat with her back very straight, as if poor posture were a moral failing, and old Peyam beside her with his prayer beads wound around one wrist, his eyes half-closed even before the naming started. Suna was behind them with Kael and Rimi and Asha, organizing children's positions with small efficient gestures, as if braiding hair and steering a crisis used the same part of her hands. The Daos sat in the middle: Hala always with both hands flat on the bench beside her, half-ready to stand, a stance built by forty-four years of getting between her younger sister and trouble. Bett next to her, arms crossed, face closed. The Leos took the back wall. Cade kept Pik between himself and Wynn, a quiet triangle of protection that Leni had noticed from the first day — the way Cade's hand went to Pik's shoulder when anyone official spoke, the way Wynn leaned slightly toward the boy as if proximity were a form of legal claim. Vera, Cade's mother, sat at the end of their bench, a gray-haired woman with steady hands who carried a sewing needle in her front pocket and used it the way other people used worry beads — Leni had watched her mend three separate garments since they'd arrived, always with tiny, precise stitches, always while listening to something she wasn't supposed to comment on.

Jin and Melie sat wherever there was room, which these days meant near the Daos. Since the allergic reaction, Bett checked every plate before Melie touched it. Jin accepted it with stiff courtesy. The thanks sat on his face without making it to his mouth; the words looked too expensive.

The Okoyes sat wherever Bram decided to sit. Today, that was the front.

Leni clocked it immediately. Her father usually chose the middle — close enough to engage, far enough to observe, the teacher's instinct for sight lines. Front-row Bram was argument-Bram. She'd seen the posture at parent-teacher nights, at the town-council meeting he'd dragged her to when she was twelve, at the kitchen table when her parents argued about whether to leave the city. His shoulders came forward and his chin dropped and his hands went into motion, the left circling loosely while the right chopped air for emphasis. She'd once timed the hand-chops at a school-board meeting: three per sentence when he was warming up, five or six when he meant it.

Her mother sat beside him with Ada on her chest. Nessa's face wore the careful blankness that meant she'd tried to talk him out of whatever this was and failed. She was picking lint off Ada's blanket in small, methodical strokes — the cleaning that wasn't really cleaning, just something to do with her hands while she waited for the argument she couldn't prevent.

Fasa came in from the custodians' walkway, followed by Olet. She carried the naming ledger — cloth-bound, water-stained along the spine — held against her chest with both hands, the grip of someone who had carried this same object a thousand times and no longer noticed its weight. She took her place at the center of the room and waited.

The conversations thinned. They always did. Fasa had a quality of silence that worked better than volume — she didn't shush anyone, didn't raise a hand, just stood there with her attention aimed at the room like a lens, and people stopped talking because the quiet she carried was heavier than whatever they'd been saying.

"The naming gathers us," Fasa said. "We speak what is true. We hear what is spoken."

Leni could have said it in her sleep by now. The formula was always the same. What changed was what came after.

Her father stood up.

The room noticed. Standing during the opening was new. Fasa paused with the ledger half-open, her eyes going to Bram with the focused attention of someone who had, perhaps, been waiting for this.

"Before we continue," Bram said, "I want the room to consider what we're being asked to do."

His voice was steady — the teacher's voice, calibrated to carry without shouting, clear enough to reach the back wall where the Leos sat. Leni watched his left hand begin its circle.

"Nine days ago, the road flooded and we came here because there was nowhere else to go. The custodians took us in. They've fed us, given us shelter, and I'm grateful for that — genuinely grateful."

The *genuinely* was his setup word. She'd heard it a hundred times. It meant the reversal was two sentences away.

"But the naming isn't hospitality. It's a requirement. Every day we stand in this room and declare our families — our composition, our relationships, our private circumstances — in front of strangers. And each time the questions go deeper. Who your spouse is. Where they are. Whether your children are biologically yours. Whether the family you've described is the family you actually have."

He paused. Jin's face went still — a quick tension around his eyes, then nothing, the careful blank he'd been wearing for nine days. Across the room Cade shifted on the bench and rested his arm along the wall behind Wynn, a motion so deliberately casual it announced itself.

"Outside this shrine," Bram continued, and his voice picked up the slightly faster cadence Leni recognized as conviction — not anger yet, but the gathering force of an argument he'd been building for days — "there is a government requiring identity papers. Ethnic registration. Movement permits tied to documented family structure. People are being stopped at checkpoints and made to prove who they are, who they belong to, whether their papers match their faces. That's why most of us are here. We were trying to get away from exactly that system."

His right hand chopped once. Twice.

"The naming is the same impulse in different clothing. It asks the same questions the state asks. It requires the same public declarations. And it carries the same implicit threat — cooperate or face consequences. The only difference is the state uses soldiers and checkpoints, and the shrine uses" — he paused, choosing — "tradition."

The room held its breath. Not because the argument was shocking — Leni suspected most of the adults had thought some version of it — but because someone had finally said it aloud, in front of the custodians, with his hands moving and his teacher-voice carrying and his glasses catching the grey light from the open wall.

Bram turned to Olet. The head custodian stood where he always stood — slightly apart from Fasa, near the stone pillar, with his hands at his sides and his face arranged in the same expression it always held, which was no expression at all. Olet's face was a policy. It did not change for arguments, for children crying, for the sound of the flood below them. It did not change now.

"I'm asking you to address this directly," Bram said. "Not the formula, not the tradition. The substance. Why should these families be required to declare their private business to you?"

Olet was silent for a moment. Not a dramatic silence — Leni had learned to distinguish Olet's silences from the pauses people used for effect. His were simply empty. The space where urgency should have been, and wasn't.

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said. "Families who come to the shrine participate in the naming. This has been so for longer than I have served here, and for longer than anyone living remembers."

"That's not an answer," Bram said. "That's a description. I'm asking you to justify — "

"The naming does not require justification. It requires participation."

Leni watched her father's hands stop moving. Just for a second. Then the left one started again, faster.

"You don't hear how that sounds? You genuinely don't hear yourself?"

"I hear you clearly, Bram Okoye. You have spoken. I have answered."

The thing was — and this was what Leni couldn't stop tracking — her father was right. His argument lined up in her head the way geometry proofs did: each box filled, each reason following the last. Same questions, same demand to show your work. Only here the chalkboard was a ledger and getting it wrong could cost you dinner.

But Olet wasn't arguing. He was talking the way the bus driver back home said *last stop*: not angry, not persuasive, just done. Her father kept reaching for a seam and there wasn't one. The whole thing was welded shut.

"If a family refuses to participate," Bram said — and now his voice was quieter, which Leni recognized as danger, the dropping register that meant he was about to ask the question he already knew the answer to — "what happens?"

"They may stay," Olet said.

"And?"

"They may stay."

"That's it? No consequences?"

Olet's pause was longer this time. A fraction of a second, maybe two. Leni noticed because she'd been timing his pauses since the second day — not consciously, at first, but then as a kind of practice, a way to measure what the man didn't say by how long he didn't say it.

"The naming and the common stores are one provision," Olet said. "The shrine offers both together. Families who do not participate in the naming may continue to stay at the shrine. They cannot draw from the common food stores."

The room changed. Not the sound — the quality of the silence shifted, from attention to arithmetic. Leni watched it happen on face after face: people counting mouths, counting days, counting what they had left in their bags. Suna uncrossed her arms and put one hand on Rimi's shoulder, which was not comfort but inventory — the automatic gesture of a woman confirming her children were still in reach. Venn looked down the row of Kondos — the quick, assessing scan of a man counting the mouths he was responsible for. Tila's thumb went still on her wedding ring. Hala's hands pressed harder against the bench. Nessa pulled Ada closer and the baby made a small sound of protest at the tightening.

"You're saying you'll starve us into compliance," Bram said.

"I am saying what the shrine provides and the terms on which it provides it."

"That's coercion. You understand that? You're using food as — "

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said, and the repetition landed with a flatness that was worse than any rebuttal. He had said what he was going to say. He would go on saying it. The room could object as long as it wanted and his answer would not change, because it was not an answer — it was the shape of the wall.

Fasa had not moved. She stood with the ledger against her chest and her eyes on Bram, and for a moment Leni saw something cross her face that she couldn't quite name. Not agreement with Bram. Not disagreement with Olet. Something more private — a flinch, almost, as if the mechanism she'd served for twenty years had been opened in front of her and the workings were uglier than she'd remembered. It lasted half a second. Then Fasa's face settled back into the custodian expression — calm, attentive, present — and the moment was gone.

"If there are no further questions," Fasa said, "the naming will continue."

"There are further questions," Bram said.

"They will be heard. The naming hears what is spoken."

Bram looked at Fasa, then at Olet, then at the room. His left hand closed and opened. He pushed his glasses up with one knuckle and pressed his fingers against his eyelids — the gesture he made at home when the internet failed for the third time in a week, not dramatic, just the specific exhaustion of fighting something that wouldn't acknowledge the fight.

Nessa reached up and touched his hand. Just that. Not pulling him down, not squeezing — her fingers against the back of his wrist, and Leni could read the whole conversation in the touch: *sit down, this isn't working, the baby needs to eat, you've said what you came to say.*

He sat down.

&nbsp;

Hala was the first to speak after the custodians left. She waited until the covered walkway's door closed behind them, then said, "Well, shit," in a flat voice that did more work than Bram's entire argument.

Nobody suggested leaving the hall. Moving would have meant the conversation was over, and nobody was ready for that — even if nobody knew what to do with it while it was still happening.

"We don't comply," Bram said. He was leaning forward, elbows on his knees, glasses pushed up into his hair. Without the glasses his face looked younger and more uncertain, which was something Leni did not want to see right now. "All seven families refuse together. If we refuse collectively, they can't enforce it. They're five people. We're forty-three."

"We're forty-three people who can't leave," Suna said from the Mera side of the room. "That's not the same as forty-three people with options."

"If we refuse as a body — "

"We are not a body," Venn said. He didn't raise his voice. He never needed to; the room leaned toward it anyway. "We are seven families. My family is twelve. How long do you propose we hold out?"

He didn't wait for an answer.

"I will tell you how long. Our provisions lasted until the fifth day. Everything we've eaten since then has come from the common stores. Mava is seventy-three. Ori is nine. There are four other children under sixteen in my family alone. If you want to stand on principle, Okoye, stand on it. But not with my family's meals."

"And that's exactly what they're counting on," Bram said. His sentences were getting longer, the subordinate clauses multiplying — Leni could hear the teacher in him building the argument even as the argument was losing, still believing a well-constructed case should matter. "The leverage works precisely because the larger families can't afford to resist. Which means the larger families are being punished for — "

"Don't lecture me about my family's size."

"I'm not — "

"My family is not your argument."

"Our provisions are gone too," Suna said, cutting between them with the neutral efficiency she brought to everything — settling children, distributing blankets, stopping fights before they fully committed. "We have the children's emergency food. Dried fruit, some biscuits. Two days' worth if I stretch it. Maybe three."

"The Parras have nothing," Hala said. She looked toward the back of the hall where Jin stood against the wall with Melie beside him. Jin gave a nod so slight it was more a contraction of muscle than a gesture. Melie was braiding and unbraiding a strand of her own hair with one hand, the other locked in Jin's, her face carrying the particular opacity of a child who knows the adults are talking about something bad and has decided not to ask.

"We've got some things," Hala continued. "Dried fish, some oil. Bett packed for a longer trip than the rest of us." She didn't look at Bett as she said it. Bett sat with her arms folded, face in its public setting: guarded, level, offering nothing she hadn't chosen to offer. "But it's for four. Not forty-three. And it won't last."

Vera Leo's needle paused in the cloth of Pik's shirt. She didn't resume sewing.

"We'll sit tomorrow," Cade said. He set each word down like a foot on rotten wood, checking it before he trusted it. "I'm not pretending we won't. But Pik doesn't answer for us. He says his name if they ask it. That's all."

Wynn kept one hand on the boy's knee. "If they want the part we don't know, they ask us. They don't get it out of him."

"He's seven," Vera said. "They can do their own dirty work."

Jin spoke from the wall without moving off it. "Same for Melie. If they want answers from my family, they ask me. She doesn't open her mouth."

That was the first real count of the room, Leni thought. Not her father's forty-three against five. This was smaller and meaner: the Leos closing around Pik, Jin bracing around Melie, each family deciding which body took the question.

"Then start there," Suna said. "No child answers. Not Melie, not Pik, not any of mine. Children stay behind the adults. We keep our answers short and we don't hand over more than they ask."

Bram looked at her. "You hear what we're doing."

"Yeah," Suna said. "I'm trying to keep doing it with fed children."

"Draw whatever lines you like," Venn said. "The Kondos will speak for the Kondos. But my family is eating tomorrow. We will not miss a meal because another family wants to make a stand."

Deshi had been watching from the Mera side of the room with her chin slightly raised, waiting until the room had tired itself out enough for her cut to land.

"You thought," Deshi said, and her voice had the dry, scoring quality that made Leni glad she wasn't on the receiving end, "that the custodians who feed us, house us, and control every hour of every day were offering the naming as a gentle invitation? A polite request we could decline?"

The room absorbed it. Deshi had done what a red pen did to one of Leni's essays: circled the thing that had been sitting there the whole time and made everybody feel stupid for missing it. Nine days of naming. Nine days of eating from the same stores. They'd been paying already. Bram had just said the price out loud.

"It's coercion," Bram said. He said it to the room, not to Deshi, and his voice had gone flat in a way that Leni recognized — the register he dropped into when he stopped trying to persuade and started trying to mark something permanent. "I want that stated clearly. What we're doing when we comply isn't consent. It's compliance under material duress, and I want that acknowledged."
