He looked at her and she saw the hesitation she'd learned to recognize — the jaw working before speech, the fractional pause that had become as familiar to everyone in the compound as the bell. He was holding a tin cup with both hands, the way Melie held things, as if each object deserved his full grip.

"I can — " He stopped. His teeth closed on the consonant. He looked down at the cup, then at her, then at the wet stones between them. "I can go."

"You don't need to," she said.

He didn't sit down. He stood at the edge of the courtyard, two body-lengths from the wall where Suna sat, and he looked up at the sky with an expression she couldn't read. Or maybe she could read it but didn't want to name it, because naming other people's feelings was a way of managing them and she was tired of managing.

"It stopped," he said.

"I know."

"Melie asked if that meant we could leave." His voice caught on the l. "I didn't know what to tell her."

Suna watched his hands on the tin cup. His thumbnails were bitten short, the skin around them raw — a habit she'd noticed in the first week but hadn't thought about again until now. He was shivering slightly. The evening wasn't cold enough for shivering. Something else was doing it.

"Sit down," she said. It came out the way she said things to her children: not a request, not a command, just the next practical step.

He sat on the wall, leaving the space of a person between them. He set the cup on the stone between his feet — empty, she saw now, or nearly. He'd brought it as something to hold.

For a while neither of them spoke. The compound behind them was settling. She could hear Deshi's voice from the sleeping section, the particular rhythm of her evening instructions — fold this, move that, where did Asha put her sandals. From the other direction, Bram's voice, low and indistinct, probably talking to Nessa. A moth circled the oil lamp in the passage they'd both come through, its shadow large and erratic against the stone wall.

"I watched Gitta," Jin said.

Suna waited.

"I watched her stand up and tell them about Fen. Sixteen years." He rubbed his thumb against the rim of the cup. "She didn't ask anyone to forgive her. She didn't explain why. She just said what happened."

"I know," Suna said. "I was there."

"And then Fen came back." His voice went quiet. Not quieter — she'd never heard Jin speak above a conversational volume — but the quality of the quiet changed, became something he was working to maintain. "He came back and sat next to her and didn't say anything and that was — I haven't been able to stop thinking about that."

"About Fen coming back?"

"About what she must have felt. All those years. Every time someone said he looks like his grandfather or he has your husband's hands. Sixteen years of people telling her a lie she invented and having to just — " He made a sound that wasn't a word. His jaw locked and released. "I told them Melie's mother was dead."

The sentence landed in the courtyard between them. Suna's body registered it before her mind did — her hands pressed flat against the stone, fingers spreading, and the cold of the wet surface shot up through her wrists and held her there. She didn't move. She didn't speak. She could feel the stone's grit under her fingertips, each grain of it distinct.

"She's not dead," Jin said. The words came faster now, as if he'd pulled a plug and the pressure behind it was doing the work. "She's alive. She's alive and she left. She left when Melie was four. Just — left. Packed a bag while I was at work and Melie was at the neighbor's and she was gone when I got home. She left a note on the table next to Melie's juice cup, just a note, not even a long one, just I can't do this anymore and I'm sorry and don't look for me."

Suna didn't speak. She watched his hands. They'd abandoned the cup and were gripping the edge of the wall, knuckles taut, the raw skin around his thumbnails whitening.

"I told Melie she died. I told her mama got sick and died. Because what was I supposed to — she was four. How do you tell a four-year-old that her mother looked at her and at me and at the house and the dishes in the sink and the juice cup on the table and decided — that she didn't — " His voice broke. Not dramatically, not with a sob. It broke the way a stick breaks, a clean snap, and then he was breathing hard through his nose and staring at the wet stone between his feet.

The moth's shadow moved on the wall behind them. The flood murmured below. Somewhere in the compound a child laughed — one of the Kondo girls, maybe Ori — and the sound was so ordinary and so distant that it felt like it belonged to a different place entirely.

"And then we came here," Jin said. His voice had steadied, but it was thin, scraped out. "And they started asking. And I said the same thing. She died. She got sick and she died. And every day they asked more questions and every day the lie got — I could feel it getting thinner, like cloth you've washed too many times, and then Fasa, in the naming, she asked when and where and how and I couldn't — " He swallowed. "The dates didn't work. The story I built didn't hold up because I'm not — I'm not good at this. I can't maintain it. Gitta did it for sixteen years and I can't do three months."

Suna sat very still beside him. Her hands were flat on the stone and the cold was in her wrists and the silence where the rain used to be was enormous around them. She was watching his profile — the dark circles under his eyes, the hunched angle of his shoulders in the borrowed jacket, the young face that wasn't young anymore, that hadn't been young since a woman walked out of a house and left a note next to a juice cup — and she was feeling something rise in her chest that she hadn't felt in nineteen days. Or three months. Or longer.

She recognized it. She'd been pushing it down every morning when she made the children's beds and organized the day and answered Deshi's questions and deflected Nessa's kindness and stood at the naming and spoke her family's name and composition with a voice so steady it convinced everyone including herself.

"He didn't go ahead," Suna said.

Jin turned to look at her. His face was wet and he wasn't wiping it.

"My husband." She heard her own voice, steady, factual, the voice she used for household logistics and naming declarations. "He didn't go ahead to arrange things. He left. Three months before the flood. He left."

She waited for the vertigo. It came — a dropping sensation behind her ribs, the feeling of saying aloud something she'd kept inside so long it had grown into her, become load-bearing, part of the structure she used to hold herself upright. But the vertigo was smaller than she'd expected. Or maybe she'd been falling for three months and just hadn't looked down.

"He took his mother's photograph and his second pair of shoes and he left on a Tuesday morning. Rimi had a school exam. He'd promised to walk her."

Jin was looking at her. He'd stopped gripping the wall. His hands were in his lap, open, empty.

"I told Deshi he went ahead. I told the children. I told Nessa. I told the naming." She heard the list forming, the cataloguing that was her own particular refuge — numbering the people she'd deceived as if they were tasks she could complete and check off and put behind her. "I don't know if Deshi believes it. I think she knows. I think she knew before I did. She doesn't ask about him. She asks about everything — where are Asha's sandals, why is the rice burned, did I comb Kael's hair this morning — but she doesn't ask about him. That's how I know she knows."

"Suna."

"Don't," she said, and the word was sharper than she intended. "Don't say you're sorry. I can't do anything with that right now."

He nodded. They sat in the silence together.

The clouds had thinned further. Above the western edge of the compound wall, three stars were visible — faint, tentative, as if they weren't sure the sky would hold. Suna couldn't remember the last time she'd seen stars. Nineteen days of cloud cover, and before that she hadn't been looking up. Before that she'd been packing what the children could carry and selling what they couldn't and telling Rimi they were going on an adventure and telling Kael his father would be there when they arrived and telling Asha nothing because Asha was four and absorbed whatever atmosphere surrounded her, which lately had been the atmosphere of a household quietly dissolving.

"How old is Melie?" she asked, and the question surprised her as it left her mouth. She knew how old Melie was. She'd watched her play with Asha and Kael every day for nearly three weeks.

"Six," Jin said. "She was four when — " He didn't finish.

"Asha was nearly four when he left." Suna looked at the three stars. "She'll remember pieces. His sandals by the door. The sound of him clearing his throat in the mornings. Not him, not the way Kael will. Rimi remembers everything."

"Does Rimi know?"

"I don't know." The admission cost her something — the first time she'd said it to anyone. "She hasn't asked. She's twelve. She's old enough to know that not asking is its own kind of answer."

Another star appeared. Then two more. The sky was clearing from the west, the monsoon cover pulling apart, and the exposed sky behind it was deep and scattered with light. Suna stared at it. The beauty felt almost aggressive after nineteen days of grey — intrusive, like a guest arriving too early and too cheerfully to a house still in mourning.

"I watched Gitta too," she said. "And what I kept thinking was that her lie was sixteen years old and mine is three months and hers was about taking a child and mine is about losing a husband and they're not the same. They're not comparable. But the weight — " She stopped. She wasn't used to speaking this way, reaching for emotional language. It felt foreign in her mouth, clumsy, like using the wrong tool for a familiar task. "Every morning I wake up and the first thing I think is remember the story. Before I think about breakfast or Deshi's mood or the naming. Remember the story. He went ahead. He's arranging things. He'll be there when we arrive. And then I have to maintain it all day. And I'm tired."

She said it simply, without drama. I'm tired. The plainest sentence in any language.

Jin reached for his empty cup and held it. She watched him turn it in his hands — a slow rotation, the metal catching what little lamp light reached the courtyard from the passage behind them.

"Melie knows something's wrong," he said. "She doesn't ask about her mother anymore. She used to ask. Every day, for months. When's mama coming back, where did mama go, is mama in heaven. I said yes, heaven, and she stopped asking. But she didn't stop knowing." He pressed his thumb against the cup's rim. "The allergy thing — when Bett saved her — Melie was more scared of me than of the reaction. Because she saw my face and my face said I can't do this. I can't keep her safe. I don't know how to do this alone."

"You're keeping her safe," Suna said.

"I'm lying to her every day."

Suna had nothing to offer that. She sat with it. The stone wall under her was losing its chill, warming toward the dry roughness she remembered from daylight hours, from afternoons when the rain paused and the stone absorbed the sun's heat and held it.

From the main hall, the custodians' bell rang once — not the insistent triple peal of a naming summons but the single tone that marked the end of communal hours. Settle in. Lamps out within the hour. The sound was so familiar now that Suna's body responded before her mind did: she straightened, began the mental list. Check that Asha is on her mat, that Kael's sandals are by the door, that Peyam has water for the night, that —

She stopped the list. Let it go. The children were with Deshi. They would be managed. She could sit here for a few more minutes without the Mera family collapsing.

"Gitta didn't ask anyone's permission," Jin said. "She didn't wait for Fasa to drag it out of her. She didn't let the naming break her open. She chose."

"She chose," Suna agreed.

More stars came through by the minute. The break in the clouds had spread across half the western sky, and the stars no longer looked tentative; they burned with the blunt, ordinary confidence of things that had been there all along, hidden only by weather. The sheen was draining off the flagstones.

"I'm going to tell the naming the truth," she said.

She hadn't known she was going to say it until she said it. Or maybe she had. Maybe she'd known since Gitta stood up. Maybe she'd known since Nessa told her about Efe and Suna realized that everyone in this compound was carrying something dead they wouldn't name.

"This isn't for them," she said. "Fasa can make of it what she wants. Olet can keep saying what he likes about the road." She looked at the stars, not at Jin. "Because the lie is heavier than the truth. I've been carrying it for three months and it's heavier than anything else I'm carrying and I'm carrying a lot."

Jin didn't say anything for a long time. The hush in the courtyard kept widening; even the flood sounded farther away, and above them the gap in the clouds kept opening.

"What will you say?" he asked.

"That he left. That he's not waiting for us. That the Mera family is five people, not six." She heard her own voice and it sounded different — not the competent managing voice, not the naming-declaration voice, but something underneath both. Something that had been waiting. "And Deshi will close her eyes and Rimi will look at me and Kael will ask where papa went and Asha won't understand and it'll be true in the room, not just true in my head."

"And then everyone will know."

"Everyone already suspects. I'm the worst liar in this compound. I just — I thought being competent was the same thing as being convincing." She almost laughed. The sound she made wasn't quite laughter, but it had the shape of laughter, the brief exhalation, the way her shoulders moved. "It's not."

Jin set the cup on the stone. He looked at her and his face in the starlight was the face of a man who had just spoken more in twenty minutes than he had in nineteen days. The exhaustion of it was visible — in his posture, in the way his hands lay open and spent on his knees, in the dark eyes that were no longer guarding anything.

"I can't," he said. "Not yet. I can't tell her. Not in the naming, not with everyone watching. She's six."

"I know."

"If I tell her that her mother chose to leave — if I take away died and give her left — she'll think it was her fault. Kids do that. They think everything is about them because everything is about them. Their whole world is the size of their house and their parents and if one of those things disappears they think they made it disappear."

"I know," Suna said again. "I have three of them."

He looked at the stars. She watched him look at them — the way his head tipped back, the exposed line of his throat, the borrowed jacket bunching at his shoulders.

"Tell her before the naming," Suna said.

He looked at her.

"Tell her yourself. Before they make you. Because they will — Fasa, or the naming, or the road opening, or Melie growing old enough to put it together on her own. Someone will make you tell her. So do it yourself, in your own words, holding her. Not in a room full of forty people. Not with Fasa asking when and where and how. Just you and Melie and the truth."

His jaw worked. The catch, the lock, the release. He kept looking at the stone between his shoes.

"I don't know how," he said.

"You just told me. You found the words for me. You can find them for her."

"That's different. You're not six."

"She's not four anymore either. You said it yourself — she's stopped asking. That doesn't mean she believes you. It means the story hurts. It'll hurt worse if the naming rips it open in front of everybody."

He put his face in his hands. His shoulders curved in, the jacket bunching around his neck, and he sat like that — bent, folded, his breathing loud in the quiet courtyard. Suna sat beside him and did not touch him and did not speak and did not manage. She let him sit with it. She let herself sit with her own decision, the weight of it — not heavier than the lie, she realized, but differently heavy. A weight she'd chosen rather than one she'd been dragging.

The last dampness had gone out of the courtyard. Heat breathed up from the flagstones, and the sky above was thick with stars she hadn't seen in nineteen days. The flood below had dropped into the background, stripped for a moment of catastrophe and reduced to water moving in the dark.

"If the road opens," Jin said from behind his hands.

"When it opens."

"When it opens." He lowered his hands. His face was blotched, his eyes red, and he looked his age — twenty-nine, which was too young for this, though Suna wasn't sure what age would be old enough. "Where are you going?"

She hadn't thought about it. She'd been so focused on the daily management of being here that she'd stopped thinking about a destination, a city she'd been traveling toward, housing she'd been promised by a cousin who might or might not still be expecting them. Her husband was supposed to be there. He wasn't there. She didn't know where he was.

"Forward," she said. "The same direction. Just without the story about who's waiting."

They sat together without touching, both of them facing the open square of sky. They were two parents on a stone wall in a shrine courtyard on a hillside above a flood, each with a child asleep one wall away and a story about to split open by morning.

Suna listened to the place the rain had vacated. The silence carried no message. It was only the clean gap left when water stopped striking stone. Weather had simply stopped.

But the stars were real, and the air was drying, and somewhere in the sleeping quarters her children were on their mats with Deshi folding blankets over them, and tomorrow she would stand in the naming and say what was true and the room would hear it and Deshi would close her eyes and Rimi would look straight at her and Kael would ask his question in that clear carrying voice of his.

She could force the words out. She couldn't stop what came after.

"Jin," she said.

He looked at her.

"It's not your fault either."

He opened his mouth and closed it. The jaw-lock held, then released into nothing. He looked at her. She didn't nod or look away.

Then he picked up his empty cup and stood. He was unsteady for a moment — the wall was low and his legs had stiffened — and she almost reached out to steady him but didn't. He found his balance.

"Thank you," he said. The hesitation on the th, the slight elongation, and then the rest coming out clean. "For listening. And for — " He stopped. Shook his head. "Thank you."

"Go check on Melie."

He nodded and walked back through the passage, his footsteps careful on the drying stone, the borrowed jacket too wide across his shoulders. She watched him go.

Suna sat alone in the courtyard. She tilted her head back and looked at the sky, which was deep and dark and alive with more light than she could count. The compound was almost silent now. One oil lamp still burned in the passage. The moth was gone. She could hear, very faintly, the sound of someone humming — Mava, maybe, one of her old songs, the melody drifting from the Kondo sleeping section. Or maybe it was Kiri Tesh, humming the lullaby that had come from somewhere none of them could trace. She couldn't tell from here. It was just a thread of sound in the quiet.

She stood up from the wall, and it left its warmth along the backs of her thighs. In nineteen days the compound had printed itself into her body — rough stone, narrow passages, the give of a mat on the floor — a place she hadn't chosen and couldn't leave and had somehow come to know by feel.

Tomorrow she would tell the truth. Tonight she would check on her children and lie on her mat listening to Deshi breathe on the other side of the partition, listening for Kael to turn over, for Rimi to stay awake longer than she admitted, and wait for morning without building the story back up.

She walked back through the passage toward the sleeping quarters. The lamp flame bent as she passed. Behind her, the prayer courtyard lay open to the sky — stone and starlight and the low murmur of the flood below. Ahead, behind the thin partition, her family was sleeping inside the last night of the lie.


# Chapter 17

Her father's mat was already empty, folded at the corner. Leni registered that before she registered the quiet.

Ada was asleep on Nessa's chest, breathing in the tiny percussive rhythm that had become the Okoye section's metronome. Tomas was curled on his side, one arm thrown over his face. Nothing unusual. But the air was different — thinner somehow, or clearer, and the compound sounded like someone had lifted a blanket off the world.

She sat up and pulled her braids over her shoulder. The stone sill under the sleeping quarter's narrow window was dry. She pressed her palm against it and felt heat, actual sun-stored heat, where for weeks there had been only the damp cool of perpetual wet.

The rain had stopped.

She'd known this was coming. Last night the dripping from the overhang had slowed and then quit, and she'd lain there listening to the new quiet push into the spaces where water sounds had lived for nineteen days. But sleep had softened the strangeness, and now the absence hit her freshly. She could hear a bird. She hadn't heard a bird in almost three weeks. The note was small and repetitive and impossibly clear, as though the creature had been waiting behind the weather this whole time.

Through the window she could see the sky — not the low grey compress of the monsoon but actual sky, a pale washed blue already warm at the edges where the sun was climbing.

Her father was in the courtyard. She could see him through the doorway, standing near the cistern with his hands in his pockets and his head tilted back, looking upward with an expression she couldn't read. He looked thinner than when they'd arrived. They all did. Three weeks of custodian rations and communal cooking had sharpened every adult's face into something closer to the bone. But on her father it read as drawn rather than lean, as though the weight he'd lost had come from somewhere internal.

She bit a hangnail and went to find breakfast.

The kitchen was already busy. Deshi was stirring something in the big pot with the kind of aggressive competence that dared anyone to offer help. Hala was laying out flatbread on the stone shelf, and two of the Kondo women — Tila and Habel — were sorting the last of the dried lentils, picking out small stones with practiced fingers. Tila wore a cotton headscarf she'd rewashed so many times it had gone transparent at the temples, and she hummed something tuneless and private as she worked. The cooking smoke was the same — that pervasive wood-char smell that had seeped into every garment and every mat — but without the rain's mineral undertone, it smelled sharper, almost acrid.

Leni took a piece of flatbread and leaned against the stone wall and chewed and watched. She was good at watching. She'd always been good at it, but three weeks in the compound had turned the habit into a survival reflex; she clocked lies now by who kept their hands busy and who stared too hard at the floor. She saw things. She couldn't stop.

Like: Suna was sitting cross-legged near the kitchen edge, braiding Asha's hair, and her hands were steady but her face wasn't. She had the over-braced look of somebody who'd made up her mind in the dark — or last night, after the evening meal, when she and Jin had been sitting together in the prayer courtyard for what must have been an hour. Leni had seen them from the sleeping quarters' doorway, two shapes on the stone bench with their shoulders almost but not quite touching, speaking in voices too low to carry. She hadn't tried to listen. She'd been doing too much of that already.

The bell rang.

Not the naming bell — a shorter, sharper pattern that Olet used for general assembly. The sound cut through the new quiet with a metallic authority that made Leni's teeth ache. Around the kitchen, hands paused. Deshi's spoon hovered over the pot. Habel looked up from her lentils.

They gathered in the main hall, all of them, the way they always did when the bell demanded it — families clustering together by reflex, the children herded to the front, the custodians standing near the hall's open side where the valley spread below. And Leni saw it then, what the clear sky had been trying to tell her all morning: the water was lower. Not dramatically, not a sudden biblical recession, but the brown surface that had swallowed the road and the valley floor for nearly three weeks had pulled back. She could see the first switchback. Mud-slicked and littered with debris, but visible — actual road surface, grey under the new sun.

Olet stood with his hands clasped at his waist, unhurried as always, as though the news he carried were no different from a change in the meal rotation. Fasa was behind him, and Leni thought she looked tired. Not sleepy — depleted. Whatever private reckoning the custodians had been conducting in their quarters, it had left Fasa wrung out.

"The radio received a broadcast this morning," Olet said. His voice was the same: level, measured, the institutional calm that had unsettled Leni since the first day. "Road crews are clearing the route from the lowlands. The pass should be open within two days."

The room inhaled. Not a gasp — the dry, collective pull of breath people make when hope barges in before they're ready to trust it, reshaping posture and expression as it went. Leni saw her mother pull Ada closer. She saw Venn straighten, his broad shoulders squaring, his chin lifting with the specific relief of a man who had been enduring something beneath him. She saw Mava's hand go to her ear, the old circular rub, and then drop. She saw Jin look at Melie, who was sitting between the Okoye and Dao children with her legs crossed and her hands busy with a string game Raya had taught her, unaware that the sentence just spoken had changed everything.

"Two days," Venn said. He didn't ask it. He confirmed it, his voice carrying the weight of a man already planning the journey to his son's wedding.

"If the crews maintain pace." Olet paused — the long, unhurried pause that Leni had learned was not hesitation but emphasis. "The road will be passable. Whether you walk it depends on the naming."

The silence that followed was a different texture than the morning's. This one had edges.

"The naming must be completed before any family departs the shrine," Fasa said. She spoke carefully, as though placing each word on a surface that might not hold. "This has always been the condition. It does not change because the road opens."

"You can't be serious." That was Bram — her father, already leaning forward, already building. Leni watched his hands come out of his pockets. "We've been here three weeks. We've done the naming. Multiple times. Your condition has been met."

"The naming has been attempted," Olet said. "It has not been completed."

"And whose standard determines that? Yours?" Bram's voice was sharper now, the teacher's diction cutting through. "You've moved the goalposts every time we've complied. First it was declaration. Then it was standing together. Then it was the losses. What's the completion criterion — when you say so?"

Olet regarded him with the same steady, impervious calm he'd maintained through every challenge. "When each family has declared truthfully."

The word threaded the room, tugging people out of whatever posture they'd arranged for themselves — Suna's sudden stillness, Jin's hand going to the back of his neck, Gitta's quiet nod that was almost invisible and entirely unsurprised.

"We leave in two days," Venn said, rising. "We do whatever you require. Tomorrow morning, first light. My family will declare exactly as they declared the first time, and then we walk."

"With respect," Fasa began, but Venn was already talking over her.

"We've been patient. We've complied. We've eaten your food and slept on your floors and tolerated —" He caught himself, the thick muscles in his neck working. "We're leaving. Name the conditions and we'll meet them, but we are leaving."

Leni looked at Arash. He was standing behind his father, slightly to the side, in the gap between the Kondo cluster and the rest of the room. His face kept trying on the controlled blankness Leni had been seeing more and more since the night she'd caught him in the courtyard with Bett, and it never quite took. Bett was across the hall, seated with Hala and Nori, and she wasn't looking at Arash. She was looking at her own hands.

---

The debate happened after the morning meal, which was itself a strange, compressed affair — everyone eating fast, as though the food had changed flavor now that departure was possible. Leni ate her lentils and watched the room rearrange itself. Alliances that had formed over three weeks were suddenly being tested against the original question: who are you, and who are you leaving with?

They gathered again in the main hall. This time there were no sleeping mats to sit on — the morning light poured through the open side, warm and foreign, and people stood or crouched on the stone floor in clusters that mapped their positions before anyone spoke.

Her father started. Of course he did.

"I want to be clear about what's happening." Bram had moved to the center of the hall, not because he was performing but because that was where the argument needed to be. His glasses caught the light and his hands were already moving, shaping the logic in the air. "For three weeks, we've been held here. The flood was real, yes. The political crisis is real. But the naming — the condition that we can't leave until we've named ourselves to the custodians' satisfaction — that was always coercion. We said so at the time. The food-access restriction confirmed it. And now the road is opening." He looked around the room. "We don't owe them a final performance."

"It's not a performance." Suna's voice came from the left side of the hall where she was standing with her children around her — Rimi beside her, Kael against the wall, Asha on Deshi's hip. She spoke without heat but with a directness that Leni had watched harden over three weeks. "The first naming was the performance. We all stood there and said whatever would get us through it. I know I did."

Bram turned to her. "That's exactly my point. The coercion —"

"I'm not talking about coercion, Bram. I'm talking about what I said." She paused. Asha was tugging at Deshi's collar, and Deshi shifted her without breaking her attention from Suna. "I lied in the first naming. About my husband. I said he was ahead of us, that we'd meet him at the next town. He left. Three months before we arrived here, he packed a bag and left."

The room went quiet in a specific way — not shocked, Leni thought, but confirmed. As though several people had suspected this and were now receiving permission to know it.

Bram opened a hand toward her, teacher-gentle now instead of argumentative. "You shouldn't have to say that to get down a road."

"No," Suna said. "I shouldn't. I'm still going to say it."

"I'm going to amend my declaration," Suna continued. "Not because the custodians require it. Because I've been carrying it for three months and three weeks on top of that, and it's heavier than the truth. So I'll say it tomorrow, in the naming, in front of everyone. And that's not compliance." She looked at Bram. "That's my decision."

Leni watched her father absorb this. His argument about coercion had assumed a unified front — all families refusing together, the way he'd tried in the food-stores standoff. But Suna wasn't refusing. She wasn't complying either. She was taking the whole argument out of his neat categories and making it personal.

"Suna's right that we all lied," Nessa said quietly. She was holding Ada against her shoulder, bouncing her with the automatic rhythm of a woman who'd been soothing babies for fifteen years. Her free hand found Bram's elbow. "Or at least we all left things out."

Bram looked at her. "This isn't the same."

Nessa's fingers tightened on his sleeve. "It is if we want tomorrow to mean anything."

Leni saw her father's face change. Not much — a small tightening around the eyes, a shift in how he held his weight. Whatever Nessa meant by we all left things out, it carried a private freight that Leni couldn't parse. She filed it away.

"The question isn't whether to do the naming." Venn was still standing, his arms crossed, his voice cutting across the murmur that had followed Suna's admission. "The question is how fast. We declare tomorrow. We leave tomorrow. Anyone who wants to amend whatever they said before, fine. But we don't drag this out. My son has a wedding."

"Your son's wedding isn't the only consideration here." Hala's voice came from the Dao corner, and it carried a different register than Venn's bark or Bram's argument — steadier, lower, with the particular authority of someone who had been listening and was now done listening. Hala was standing with her arms at her sides, her solid frame planted, her chin up. She had a bruise on her forearm from catching the corner of the cooking pot two days ago, and she didn't seem to notice it. "Nobody's saying we should stay longer than we have to. But if we're going to stand up there and declare, we should at least be honest about what's happened."

"What's happened," Venn repeated flatly.

"Ori," Hala said. "Ori, who screamed at rainwater."

"She had a bad night."

Hala took one step forward. "She recoiled from a puddle like it was pulling her under. Don't call that a bad night."

Arash moved as if he might speak. Venn's hand shot back without his even turning his head and closed around his son's forearm for a second before letting go.

"Ori," Hala said again, looking at Venn now instead of Bett. "Who never screamed at water in her life until she came here and started carrying the fear of a boy who drowned. My nephew." She looked at Bett, who gave a single small nod. "Tomas, who talks with a stutter he didn't have three weeks ago. Asha, who rubs her ear like a woman she'd never met. Kiri, who sings a song from a region she's never been to. Pik, who prays with his hands like Gitta." She turned back to Venn. "You want to declare the same thing you declared the first time? Go ahead. But you'll be lying, and everybody in this room will know it."

Venn's face darkened. His jaw worked, the way it always did when he was being challenged — the physical machinery of pride grinding against the pragmatic recognition that the room was not on his side. "What happened to our children doesn't mean —"

"It means something." Hala's voice didn't rise. It dropped, and the drop carried more force than shouting. "I don't know what it means. Nobody does. Maybe the custodians are right and maybe they're wrong and maybe it doesn't matter. But the connections are real. My sister's dead son is alive in your niece's body. That's not a theory. That's Ori screaming at a puddle."

The room was very still. Leni could hear the bird again — the same persistent note from the morning, thin and bright against the thick silence. She could hear Asha making a small, contented sound against Deshi's neck. She could hear her own pulse in her ears, which was stupid, which was exactly the kind of body-inventory she'd mock in a novel but which was happening anyway.

Cade Leo spoke then. He'd been standing near the back wall with Wynn beside him and Vera slightly behind — the three of them forming the same protective triangle they always formed around Pik, who was sitting on the floor near Melie, his small hands still in his lap. Cade was not a loud man. Leni had spent three weeks observing him and what she'd mostly observed was vigilance — a constant low-grade alertness about Pik, about the naming, about how other families would categorize his family. He wore the same thin cotton shirt he'd been rewashing since they arrived, the collar starting to fray, and when he stepped forward his movement was careful, as though he were navigating a room full of breakable things.

"What does the naming mean for a child whose origins are unknown?" He directed the question at Fasa, but the whole room heard it. "Pik is my son. Wynn's son. Vera's grandson. That's not a declaration we're prepared to amend. But he came to us from an institution with records we couldn't verify, and now he's —" He stopped. Wynn put a hand on his back. "He's doing Gitta's prayer gesture. He's never seen her pray. We keep him away from the naming. He started doing it in his sleep."

"Exactly," Bram said, turning half toward him. "That's what coercive systems do. They take uncertainty and —"

Cade didn't even look at him. "Bram, I'm not asking for a theory. I'm asking if this rite can be used against my boy."

Gitta, who had been sitting near the Tesh corner with her particular elderly stillness, turned her head toward Pik. The movement was slow and deliberate, the way everything Gitta did was slow and deliberate. Leni thought about what this woman had carried — sixteen years of knowing that Fen was not her blood, sixteen years of perfect composure, and then the confession that had cracked the compound open. Gitta's truth was already told. She had nothing left to declare except the truth she'd already spoken, and that exemption gave her a strange calm that Leni found almost enviable.

"The boy is yours," Gitta said. Her voice was the same unhurried, careful instrument it always was, each word arriving as though it had been considered and approved before departure. "The naming does not take him from you. Nothing takes a child from the person who chose to raise him." She paused. Fen, sitting beside her with his long legs drawn up and his dark hair falling across his forehead, did not move. "I know this."

Cade's mouth twitched once and his eyes went bright — gratitude and grief and the specific relief of a man whose worst fear had been spoken to directly. He nodded and stepped back. Wynn's hand stayed on his back. Vera, standing behind them both, had closed her eyes when Gitta spoke. She was a woman who wore her worry openly — three weeks of it had deepened the lines bracketing her mouth — and when she opened her eyes again she looked at Gitta with a recognition that wasn't quite gratitude but belonged to the same family. One grandmother recognizing what another had done.

Tomas tugged at Leni's sleeve. She looked down and found her brother staring up at her, his face tight with the effort of not speaking, because when he spoke now the words caught on the same consonants that caught in Jin Parra's mouth, and the catching frightened him. He'd stopped talking in group settings entirely — ten years old, sharp and talkative by nature, reduced to tugging sleeves and pointing. She put her hand on his head and felt the familiar heat of his scalp through his short hair, and something in her chest compressed.

"So what are we actually deciding?" Miro Tesh stood up, and Leni realized she'd never heard him speak in a full assembly before. He was a quiet man — broad, balding, with oil-stained hands from repairing one of the kitchen's broken stools — who'd spent three weeks absorbing the discovery that his mother had lied about his son's origins. His voice was rough, as though it hadn't been used at this volume in a while. "Are we deciding whether to do the naming? Because it sounds like most people are going to do it regardless."

"Some of us object on principle," Bram said, but the fight was leaving his voice. Leni heard it — the downward shift, the concession hidden inside the precision.

"You can object on principle and still stand up and say what's true," Suna said. "Those aren't contradictions."

Bram looked at her. Then he looked at Nessa, who was watching him with the look she used when she needed him to say the hard thing and was promising, silently, not to leave him alone in it.

"I'm not saying don't do it." Bram took his glasses off and cleaned them on his shirt, a gesture Leni recognized as his stalling mechanism. "I'm saying remember what it is. It's not a sacrament. It's not magic. It's a ritual maintained by two people who control our food supply and have told us, in so many words, that the flood won't recede until we play along. We can choose to speak truthfully and still acknowledge that the choice was coerced."

"The rain stopped," Peyam said.

Everyone turned. Peyam had been sitting on the floor near the back, his thin legs folded, his hands resting on his knees. Leni had always thought of him as furniture — one of those old men who existed at the periphery of their families, praying and dozing and taking up very little space. But his voice, when it came, was surprisingly clear and carried an old man's unselfconscious authority. He was looking at the open side of the hall, at the valley and the visible road below and the sky that had emptied itself of clouds for the first time since they'd arrived.

"The rain stopped last night," he said again. "And this morning the radio says the road is clearing. Maybe that is coincidence." He adjusted his seating on the stone floor, shifting his weight off one hip with a wince that spoke of arthritis. "Maybe."

Olet, who had been standing at the hall's edge throughout, said nothing. His silence was its own statement. Leni wished, not for the first time, that she could read the man. Three weeks, and he remained opaque to her in a way that felt deliberate — not a person hiding but a person who simply did not offer the readable surfaces that other people leaked constantly.

"Fine." Venn's voice cut through. He'd been conferring with Tila in a whispered exchange, and now he turned back to the room with the manner of a man issuing a ruling. "We do it tomorrow. My family will declare. But I want it understood: we're complying under duress. And when the road opens, we walk."

"Nobody's stopping you," Hala said.

"Nobody could," Venn said, and the raw honesty of it — the sheer patriarchal certainty — was almost impressive. Almost.

Deshi made a sound from the Mera corner — not quite a word, more the exhalation of a woman who had been holding her opinion with visible effort. She was still holding Asha, who had fallen asleep against her shoulder, and she spoke over the child's head with the quiet devastation she could deploy more effectively than shouting.

"My son left his wife and his children," she said. It was the first time Leni had heard Deshi acknowledge it aloud, and from the rigid set of Suna's shoulders, it might have been the first time Suna had heard it too. "I did not raise him to be that kind of man. But that is what he is. And if this naming wants me to say that in front of strangers, I will say it, because it is true, and because someone in this family should have said it three months ago." She looked at Suna — not with the adversarial sharpness Leni had seen for three weeks, but with the raw, uncomfortable look of a woman admitting she'd helped the lie survive and didn't know how to apologize except by naming it.

Suna didn't answer. She didn't need to. The acknowledgment sat between them like a stone set down after a long carry.

Bett had not spoken during the entire debate. Leni watched her now — the compact stillness, the guarded face, the way she sat with her hands in her lap and her eyes tracking the room without offering anything in return. Hala had spoken for her, had named Ren through Ori's fear, and Bett had allowed it with that single nod. Whatever Bett would say in the naming tomorrow, she would say it there, in the ritual space, not here in the argument about whether the ritual space was legitimate.

Arash was watching Bett. He was trying not to, and failing. Leni saw the way his gaze kept drifting across the hall and then pulling back, the way his body was angled slightly away from the Kondo cluster and toward the Dao corner, the way his jaw held the same tension his father's held but different — younger, less certain, pointed in a direction that had nothing to do with a wedding.

Fasa stepped forward. She looked like someone who had been carrying a weight that was about to shift — not lighten, but shift from her back to the families who would bear it tomorrow.

"The naming will be held tomorrow at first light," she said. "Each family will declare. Force can make a body stand in the circle; it cannot make a mouth speak plain. What is spoken tomorrow will belong to the speaker, and to whoever must hear it." She looked around the room, and Leni thought she saw ritual composure slip for a second, exposing genuine feeling, deep and tired and close to the surface. "I have conducted the naming for twenty years. I have never asked it to cut this deep. I am asking now."

Nobody answered. The bird outside sang its single note into the quiet, and after a while the families began to drift apart.

---

Evening came slowly, the first evening without rain since they'd arrived, and it was strange. The light lasted longer and fell differently — warm gold slanting through the main hall's open side, turning the stone floor amber, throwing long shadows from the pillars. The compound felt larger without the rain compressing it, as though the walls had stepped back. Sounds carried differently too: laughter from the children's corner, the scrape of the cooking pot, Venn's low voice from the Kondo section issuing instructions nobody could quite hear.

Leni sat on the courtyard wall with her journal on her knees and her pen in her hand and didn't write. She chewed the pen cap instead and watched.

Suna was in the sleeping quarters, folding her children's clothes into the two bags the Meras had arrived with. The folding was precise, methodical — shirts pressed flat, edges aligned, everything sorted by child. Leni recognized the behavior. Her own mother did this before big days, turning the domestic into the manageable, using the orderly compression of laundry as a proxy for the emotional compression she couldn't otherwise achieve. But Suna's hands paused over one of Asha's small dresses, and she held it for a moment longer than folding required, and then set it down and picked it back up and folded it a second time, exactly the same way.

Deshi came in a few minutes later with Asha on her shoulder and the smaller Mera bag hanging from two fingers. She set the bag down beside Suna with more force than necessary. From the courtyard wall Leni could see straight through the doorway. Privacy in the shrine was mostly a rumor.

"Don't put Kael's sweater at the bottom," Deshi said. "He'll need it before dawn."

Suna kept folding. "Then move it."

Deshi lowered Asha onto the mat, one hand cupping the back of the child's head until she settled. When she straightened, she did not look at Suna. "You're going to say it tomorrow."

"I said I was."

"In front of the children."

That made Suna look up. "There isn't another front."

Deshi's mouth tightened. "Then say it cleanly. Don't soften it. Don't say he was delayed or taken or that some road swallowed him."

Suna went still in the way Leni had started to recognize as dangerous. "You don't get to tell me how to name my own life."

"No," Deshi said. "I get to stop lying for my son."

The room seemed to get smaller. Even from the courtyard Leni could feel it — that pressurized quiet when two people quit circling and finally put the blade down flat between them.

"You knew," Suna said.

"I knew enough."

"Enough to let me stand in that hall and tell strangers he was ahead of us."

Deshi looked at the sleeping Asha instead of answering. When she did speak, her voice had thinned. "I kept waiting for him to come back and make a fool of me for saying it aloud."

Suna laughed once, without humor. "He already made fools of us."

Deshi reached for the little yellow dress still in Suna's lap. Suna caught her wrist before she could touch it. For a second the two women stayed like that, linked by the same scrap of cotton, neither letting go.

"I'm not taking it from you," Deshi said. "Fold it smaller. The bag won't close."

Suna released her. She refolded the dress, tighter this time, and Deshi knelt opposite her and began rolling Kael's socks into pairs with quick, irritated hands.

"I should've said it before we left home," Deshi said. "I should've said my son walked out on his wife and children and that there was nothing ahead of us but this road."

"Why didn't you?"

Deshi gave a short, dry breath that was not quite a laugh. "Because then I'd have to say I raised a man who could do it."

Suna shoved the folded dress into the bag. "You did."

Deshi nodded once. She took the answer without flinching. "Seems I did."

Asha turned in her sleep. Both women looked at her. Neither moved until the child settled again.

"Say it tomorrow," Deshi said at last, still watching Asha. "Say he left. I won't dress it up in front of everybody again."

Suna did not thank her. She slid the last shirt into place and pulled the bag ties tight while Deshi held the mouth of the bag open with both hands. It was the first time Leni had seen them work at the same thing without fighting, and it did not look anything like peace. It looked like two people bracing a split seam so it would not rip wider before morning.

By the cistern, Jin had pulled Melie up beside him on the basin rim. He kept looking at her and then out at the road. Twice Leni saw his mouth move. Twice he stopped before sound came. Melie leaned against his arm, waiting without knowing she was waiting.

Arash was alone. This was unusual — the Kondo family moved as a unit, had always moved as a unit, Venn's gravitational authority holding all twelve members in formation. But Arash was standing at the edge of the terraced garden, looking down the hill toward the road, and there was a space around him that no Kondo had filled. Leni followed his sight line and saw the first switchback, mud-covered but solid, and below it the second, still half-submerged, and below that the brown water retreating slowly from the valley floor. Two days. Two days and the road would carry them away from here, carry Arash to a wedding, to a woman waiting somewhere in a city Leni didn't know the name of, and Bett Dao would walk a different road.

Unless.

Leni bit her nail and looked away.

The custodians' quarters were quiet. The covered walkway between their section and the main compound was empty. Whatever Olet and Fasa were doing tonight, they were doing it in private — preparing for the naming, or praying, or simply sitting with the knowledge that twenty days of their work was about to resolve into something they could not predict.

She picked up her pen.

For nineteen days they'd been stuck. Now they had two days and a road and a naming that would ask them to say out loud the things they'd been carrying in private — the things the compound had broken open without anyone's permission. Bram's argument was right, the way Bram's arguments were always right: the naming was coercive, the custodians held the leverage, compliance under duress wasn't consent. But Suna's point was right too, and it cut in a direction her father's logic couldn't follow: you could be coerced into an act that you also, separately, needed to perform. The naming was both things. The truth was both things.

Hala was right. The connections were real. Tomas stuttered on consonants he'd never stuttered on before. Asha rubbed her ear in an old woman's gesture. Ori screamed at puddles. Pik prayed with Gitta's hands. None of that went away because the road opened. None of it was resolved by leaving. They would carry it with them — the Kondos to their wedding, the Parras to wherever Jin was going, the Okoyes to wherever her father's principles and her mother's fear decided to take them.

She wrote:

*Tomorrow they do the naming. The real one. Not the one where everybody stood up and said what would get them through it, but the one where it actually costs something. Deshi just told Suna not to soften what her son did. She said, Say he left. She said it like she was ripping off a bandage that had stuck. Then she got on her knees and helped pack the same bag. I couldn't tell if that was loyalty or surrender.*

*Dad's going to do it too. I know he is. Even though he spent the whole morning arguing that we shouldn't have to, he's going to stand up and say whatever Mom's been wanting him to say, whatever she meant when she said we all left things out. I don't know what they left out. I'm fifteen. There are things my parents don't tell me and I'm supposed to be okay with that but right now, tonight, with the road opening and the naming tomorrow and Tomas talking like a man he barely knows, I'm not okay with it. I'm scared.*

*Jin still hasn't said whatever he has to say to Melie. Arash is still standing on the road side of the garden like being two steps closer to the switchback might turn him into a different man. Venn's going to declare because he can't leave without declaring and he can't stay. Cade's going to hold Pik and say he's his son and mean it. Bett's going to say Ren. I've heard the name once already, but tomorrow she'll say it where nobody can pretend they missed it.*

*And Arash. God. I don't know what Arash is going to do.*

She stopped writing. The lamp beside her flickered in a breeze that smelled of wet earth and wood smoke and something green underneath — the valley exhaling after three weeks of rain. She looked up and saw her mother in the doorway of the sleeping quarters, watching her. Nessa's face was soft in the lamplight, Ada asleep on her shoulder, and for a moment Leni felt the full stupid weight of how much she loved these people who had dragged her to a shrine on a hill where the world fell apart and reformed in shapes she didn't recognize.

She looked back at her journal and wrote the last line:

*They're going to do it. And this time it's going to cost something.*

She closed the journal, capped the pen, and sat on the courtyard wall as the first stars appeared over the valley — sharp and cold and entirely indifferent to the forty-three people below them who were about to tell the truth.


# Chapter 18

The light was what Suna noticed first — not the bell, which Fasa rang at the usual hour, the same flat bronze note that had opened every naming since they arrived — but the light that came through the main hall's open side and struck the stone floor in a yellow-white band that none of them had seen in three weeks. The sun was up. It had been up yesterday too, and the day before, since the rain stopped on the evening she and Jin sat in the courtyard and told each other the same lie in reverse. But the light still startled her. It fell across the hall at a steep morning angle, and where it hit the stone it showed every scuff and water-stain of their occupation — the path worn between the entrance and the sleeping quarters, the grey smudge where the cooking fire's smoke had drifted in, the faint greasy mark near the wall where Peyam sat and ate his meals. Three weeks of living pressed into the floor.

She had Asha on her hip. The child was heavy enough now that she'd need to be put down before long, but Suna wanted the weight of her — the solid pull of a four-year-old's body against her side, the warmth of Asha's forehead against her neck. Asha's left hand was resting on Suna's collarbone, the fingers still, not rubbing her ear for once. Beside them Kael stood with his arms crossed in an imitation of Bram Okoye that he probably didn't know he was doing. Rimi was behind Deshi, close enough that her shoulder nearly touched the old woman's back. Peyam had taken his usual place near the wall, prayer beads still in his hand from the morning's devotion, his pipe tucked into his belt though there was nowhere to smoke it in the hall.

The families filed in. Suna counted them as automatically as she counted her own children, a reflex she had developed in the first days when she needed to know how many people stood between her and the entrance. The Okoyes came in together, Bram with his glasses pushed up on his forehead, Nessa carrying Ada against her chest, Tomas walking slightly apart from Leni with the sour distance of siblings who have recently argued. The Teshes: Miro carrying a folded blanket under his arm as though he expected to need it, Enn beside him, Kiri trailing behind, Gitta already seated on the stone bench against the far wall with Fen standing at her shoulder. Gitta wore the same grey shawl she had worn every day, pulled tight across her chest despite the warmth. Her hands were still.

The Daos entered from the kitchen side — Hala first, her posture protective as always, angled slightly so that her body screened Bett behind her. Kal walked with Sem, the old man's hand on the boy's shoulder in a way that was more about his knees than about affection, though maybe it was both. Raya had Nori by the hand. Bett came last, her face showing nothing.

The Leos were already seated. Cade and Wynn on the bench near the entrance, Pik between them, Vera on Cade's other side with her knitting in her lap — she'd been working on the same grey rectangle for days, something too small for a blanket and too large for a scarf, and Suna had never asked what it was. Pik was sitting on his hands, his usual tactic when the prayer gesture came on and he was trying to be still.

The Kondos were the last to enter, and they entered as they did everything — as a group, with Venn at the center and Tila half a step behind him. There were twelve of them. Mava walked with Habel, Ori close to her mother's side. Arash was at the edge of the group, not outside it exactly, but positioned like a person near a doorway he is thinking about walking through. He wore the same shirt he'd worn for three days, and the collar was frayed on one side where he'd been pulling at it. Suna had noticed this two mornings ago and hadn't thought about it since, and now she noticed it again.

Fasa stood at the hall's center, not behind the stone ledge where she usually conducted the naming but out from behind it, level with the families, on the same floor. Olet was near the open side, leaning against the stone railing, the valley behind him. From where Suna stood she could see past his shoulder to the view below: the switchback road emerging from the flood's brown margin, the lower bends still glazed with mud but visible, solid ground where for three weeks there had been only water. The waterline had dropped overnight. She could see the stone retaining wall at the third switchback, and clumps of grass and debris caught against it like a tideline.

"This is the last naming," Fasa said.

Her voice was different — not the ritual cadence, not the measured pauses of a woman who spoke liturgy daily, but plainer, as if she had set something down.

"Each family will declare. You know what the naming asks." She paused, and the pause was not ceremonial. "It asks for what is true."

The hall was quiet. Forty-three people and five custodians, and the only sound was Ada's soft breathing against Nessa's chest and the distant, diminished rush of the water below — quieter than it had been, retreating.

Deshi was behind her. Suna knew it without turning. The skin between her shoulder blades had tightened the same way it had tightened last night when, after the children slept and Peyam's snoring thickened in the dark, she told Deshi: I'm going to tell the truth tomorrow. About Sev. Deshi had smoothed the edge of her sleeping mat with her palm, once, twice, and asked nothing. She had given Suna no blessing, no objection, no rescue. The silence had stayed with Suna until morning.

Suna stepped forward.

She shifted Asha to her other hip. The child's hand came up to Suna's collar, fingers finding the seam again and worrying it as if the stitches themselves might hold.

"We are the Mera family," Suna said. Her voice carried. She had not planned it to carry — she had expected to have to push, as she did when she called the children in from the courtyard — but the stone walls did the work for her. "Deshi Mera, my mother-in-law. Peyam Mera, her brother. My children: Rimi, who is twelve. Kael, who is eight. Asha, who is four."

She paused. The weight of the next sentence was physical, sitting in her chest like a swallowed stone. It was not the words — the words were simple. She had rehearsed them in the dark, whispering to the ceiling while Asha slept against her side. The weight was in the room's reception of them, in Deshi's breathing behind her, in Rimi's face, which Suna could not see because Rimi was behind Deshi and Suna had placed her there deliberately so that she would not have to watch her daughter's expression while she said it.

"Sev Mera. My husband. Deshi's son." She said it to the hall, to the stone floor, to the view of the receding water. "He left us. Three months before we came here. He didn't go ahead. He didn't die. He left."

The silence after was not silence — it was the sound of forty people choosing not to speak, which had its own texture, its own particular quality of held breath and swallowed response. Suna felt the sweat on her palm where it pressed against Asha's back. She felt the stone floor through the soles of her shoes, warmed now by the sun that was still moving its band of light across the hall.

Behind her, Deshi closed her eyes.

Suna saw it only because she turned — she had to turn, she could not stand there facing the room and not know what was happening behind her. And Deshi's eyes were closed, and her mouth was set in the line that Suna had learned to read over thirteen years of marriage: not anger, not grief, not the sharp blade of judgment, just the closing of a door she had been holding open for three months, the acknowledgment that the thing on the other side of it was real.

Peyam's beads had stopped moving. He looked at Suna, and in his face — his old, lined, patient face with the pipe-smoke creases around his mouth — there was something she did not expect, not surprise and not sympathy but something closer to the expression of a man who has been watching a load carried poorly and is relieved to see it finally set down right.

Suna sat. She put Asha on the stone floor beside her, and Asha immediately crawled into Kael's lap, and Kael held her with the automatic competence of an eight-year-old brother who had been doing this since she was born. Rimi had not moved from behind Deshi. Suna did not turn to look at her again.

---

Jin stood before Fasa looked his way. Melie rose with him, her fist closing around his hand. He had told her last night. Suna knew because after her conversation with Deshi he had found her in the courtyard and said only: I told her. Lamplight had left his face scraped raw. Even now his free hand stayed half-curled, as if Melie's grip were still in it.

"Jin Parra," he said. The jaw caught on the first consonant, the slight lock and release that Suna had watched Tomas Okoye develop over the past two weeks, identical, borrowed, impossible. "My daughter. Melie. She's six."

He stopped. His throat worked once. Across the hall Tomas had gone still, watching Jin's mouth.

"Her mother's alive," Jin said. "She isn't with us. She left."

He sat down so quickly that Melie stumbled beside him. His hand found hers again before she'd settled, and she held on with both of hers, her face turned into his arm, not crying, not looking at anyone, just holding on.

Jin's free hand flattened on the bench beside his leg. The fingers spread hard against the stone, curled, then spread again. Tomas looked down at his own hand and then at his father.

---

Bram stood too quickly, as if he knew what would happen if the room stayed with Jin any longer. He pushed his glasses up with one finger. Nessa remained seated, Ada in her arms, but she had already shifted to the edge of the bench.

"The Okoye family," Bram said. His voice had the teacher's steadiness, the measured pace of a man accustomed to saying important things to people who needed to understand them. "I am Bram. My wife, Nessa. Our daughter Leni, who is fifteen. Our son Tomas, who is ten. Our daughter Ada, who is one."

He paused. A longer sentence had clearly been waiting behind the first one. Suna saw it gather and die. The room would not take explanation now.

"Before Tomas," he said, "there was Efe." He did not look at Nessa. He looked at the room with the hard attention of a man who is asking to be heard, not pitied. "He was our son. He lived for three months. He died."

Nessa's voice came from behind him, quiet but distinct — the voice of a woman who had lived with a name inside her for more than ten years and was now saying it where other people could hear.

"Efe," she said — just the name, once — and then she pressed her mouth against Ada's hair and went quiet.

Tomas stared at his parents as if they had opened a door in the wall behind them. His jaw caught on the first letter of nothing at all. Leni's hand came down on his shoulder, hard enough to steady both of them.

---

Gitta rose. Fen was already on his feet by the time she had one hand on the bench. She straightened without taking help, shawl pulled tight, back rigid. Miro stood half a step off her shoulder and stopped there. Enn sat with her hands knotted in her lap, the skin around her mouth drawn pale.

Gitta's voice was slow. Each word arrived at a distance from the last, weighed and released, the cadence of a woman who had spent sixteen years editing every sentence before it left her mouth.

"The Tesh family," she said. "My son, Miro. His wife, Enn. Their daughter Kiri, who is eight." She paused, built the next line in her mouth, and laid it down whole. "My grandson, Fen. He is sixteen."

She drew a breath. Suna could hear it across the hall — the slight whistle of air through old teeth, the effort of a woman who was about to say a thing she had already said days ago but which required the same force each time, as if the truth had not gotten lighter with repetition.

"Fen is not my blood," Gitta said. "Sixteen years ago I came upon a dead family at the roadside. A man. A woman. A child old enough to walk. Under the woman's arm there was an infant still breathing. I took the infant. I named him Fen Tesh. I told my son he belonged to kin. I kept that lie for sixteen years."

She did not sit. She stood in the hall's yellow-white light, small and thin and seventy years old, her hands at her sides. She had not asked for forgiveness when she confessed in the hall days ago, and she did not ask now. The naming asked for the truth. She had given it.

Fen was already standing. He did not look at Miro or Enn. He looked straight ahead, tall beside Gitta, his face blank with effort.

"I am her grandson," Fen said.

It was four words. He sat back down, and his shoulder settled against Gitta's side, and the old woman did not move toward him and did not move away. They sat like that — touching at the shoulder, looking forward, two people who had agreed on what they were to each other and did not need the agreement to be louder than this.

Miro's face tightened until the skin around his eyes shone. He had known for days and still looked struck. He sat with his forearms on his thighs and his hands clasped between his knees. Enn put her hand between his shoulder blades and left it there.

---

Cade stood before the quiet around the Teshes could harden. Wynn rose with him, shoulder to shoulder. Cade drew breath as if he might offer the long version, saw Gitta and Fen still touching at the shoulder, and let the breath out empty. Wynn's hand rested on his forearm.

"Cade Leo," he said. "My partner, Wynn. My mother, Vera." He looked down at Pik, who was sitting between Vera and the bench's stone arm, his legs swinging, his right hand moving in the slow circling pattern — Gitta's prayer gesture, thumbs turning over each other, a motion he had never been taught by anyone in his family. "My son, Pik. He's seven."

He did not say adopted. He did not mention the institution, the paperwork, the records that might or might not have been accurate. All of that stayed outside the hall. He said my son and sat down.

Vera Leo had noticed Pik's hand. She had been watching it all morning, tracking the movement as you track a bird you can't quite identify, with attention that has not yet decided whether to be worried or amazed. Now Vera reached over and took Pik's hand in hers. She didn't press his fingers flat. She didn't still the circling thumbs. She held his hand with the gesture still moving inside her grip — contained but not stopped, like holding a small animal whose heartbeat you can feel against your palm. Pik looked up at her, and Vera looked back, and the grey knitting in her lap slid an inch toward the floor, and she let it go.

---

Hala stood. She was taller than Bett and broader, and she carried herself like a woman who had spent years stepping between her sister and whatever came at her. Kal was behind her, leaning on Sem's shoulder, his face set in its usual weathered squint.

"The Dao family," Hala said. Her voice was clear, matter-of-fact, a voice accustomed to organizing. "I am Hala. My son, Sem, eleven. My daughter, Raya, thirteen. My father, Kal." She paused. "My sister, Bett. And Nori, who is seven. Nori is Bett's daughter."

Hala did not fully sit. One hand stayed braced on the bench as Bett stood.

Bett stood.

She was smaller than Hala, compact, her face showing the closed watchfulness Suna had come to recognize as Bett's permanent weather. She wore a cotton shirt with the sleeves rolled to the elbows, and her forearms were brown and scratched from the terraced garden where she'd been working the day before, pulling weeds from between the herb rows with the focused energy of a woman who needed her hands to be busy.

"Ren," Bett said.

The name hit clean. Ada startled in Nessa's arms. Peyam's beads stopped against his thumb. Ori made a small wet sound and buried her face in Habel's skirt.

"My son," Bett said. "He drowned when he was three. Seven years ago." Her voice had dropped into the dry economy of a woman who had learned that the most important things required the fewest words. "Nori came after. She isn't his replacement. She's my daughter."

Bett sat. She did not look at Hala. She did not look at anyone. Arash was looking straight at her. By the time she settled on the bench, the gap between him and the rest of the Kondos had widened by half a step.

---

Venn stood last.

He rose with his shoulders squared, chin lifted, bringing back the formal posture that had carried him through the first naming. Tila was beside him in the pressed blouse she had saved for this morning, hair combed and pinned, the effort written all over her.

"We are the Kondos," Venn said. The formal voice was back. He used it like a brace. "We are twelve. Tila, my wife. Habel, my sister. Mava, our aunt. Ori, Habel's daughter." He named the others with the same even weight, rebuilding the family aloud one brick at a time.

"Arash," Venn said. "My son."

The name hung. Arash was no longer in line with the rest of them. He stood three feet off, one shoulder angled toward the Daos, close enough to be counted and far enough that no one could pretend not to see it.

Arash did not step forward. He kept his eyes on Bett for one beat, then lowered them to the floor and spoke.

"I'm not going to the wedding," Arash said. "I won't continue."

Venn stared. In the courtyard anger had sent him forward. Here it locked him in place. Suna had seen him loud. She had never seen him mute. The silence coming off him felt heavier than his voice ever had.

Mava, seated on the bench behind Venn, reached one hand toward Arash. Her arm extended and hung in the air between them — the same arm whose gesture Asha Mera had borrowed, the slow ear-rubbing motion that had started everything. Her hand did not reach him. She held it out for a moment and then brought it back to her lap, and the gesture was complete in its incompletion, an offer that did not need to be accepted to have been made.

Tila put her hand flat on her own knee. She looked at the stone floor between her feet. Her face did not change expression because the expression she had assembled that morning — the pressed blouse, the pinned hair, the composure — was built to hold, and it held. But her hand on her knee was pressing down, the fingers white.

Venn finished. He named the remaining Kondos — the ones who were staying, the ones who would walk with him down the road to the wedding that his son would not attend. His voice did not waver. He recited the names as if nothing had interrupted, as if the family were still twelve, as if the list itself could hold its shape regardless of what the people on it chose to do with their lives. He sat. His hands went flat on his thighs, and he did not look at Arash, and Arash did not look at him, and between them was the particular silence of two men who loved each other and could not, at this moment, find a form for it that either of them could use.

---

Fasa waited. She stood in the center of the hall, in the band of sunlight that had moved since the naming began and now fell across her hands, and she waited until the room's silence had settled from the active silence of people holding their breath to the quieter silence of people who have heard what they needed to hear.

"Is the naming complete?" she asked.

She asked it to the room, not to Olet, not to any family, but to the air and the stone and the forty-three people who had been strangers three weeks ago and were not strangers now.

No one answered. The question sat in the hall for a long moment, and in that moment Suna became aware of every sound the room contained — Asha's breathing against Kael's shirt, the distant and diminished rush of water from the valley below, a bird somewhere outside the hall that she had not heard before because it had been drowned by rain for three weeks, the creak of the stone bench as someone shifted weight.

Olet spoke from his place at the railing. He had not moved during the entire naming. He stood where he had stood from the beginning, leaning against the stone with the valley behind him, and his voice was the same voice it always was — measured, unhurried, carrying no more weight than it ever carried and no less.

"The naming is complete," Olet said. "The road is open."

The road is open. Three words. Suna looked past him to the valley and saw what she had been seeing since she entered the hall but had not yet allowed herself to understand as fact: the switchback path descending the hill, muddy and littered with debris, but passable. Brown silt on the stones. Tufts of uprooted grass caught against the retaining walls. The waterline a hundred meters below where it had been when they arrived, pulled back to the valley floor like a sheet drawn down from a bed, leaving the damp, stained evidence of where it had lain.

They could leave. The thought arrived with a simplicity that felt almost absurd after everything — the namings, the manifestations, the shouting and the silence and the confessions extracted and volunteered. They could walk down the hill and onto the road and continue to wherever they had been going when the flood stopped them. The thought should have been relief, and part of it was, but the relief was layered over something else, something Suna couldn't name and didn't try to. She had spent three weeks managing collapse one task at a time, and now the tasks were different, and she was already cataloguing them: pack the bags, fill the water skins, check Asha's shoes, make sure Kael had eaten. The list was shorter than usual. The list was almost done.

Fasa looked at Olet, and Olet looked back, and between them passed something Suna could not read — an acknowledgment, a completion, the end of a process they had been conducting together for twenty years and which had, this time, asked more of everyone than it ever had before. Fasa's face was tired, not with the tiredness of a performance completed but with the tiredness of someone who has been holding a thing open for three weeks and can now let it close, and the closing takes a different kind of effort than the holding.

The families began to stand. The naming was over. There was no ceremony for the ending — no prayer, no benediction, no formal release. Fasa stepped back from the center of the hall, and Olet remained at the railing, and the families rose from the benches and the floor and began to move with the restlessness of people who have somewhere to go and have been prevented from going there and are now allowed.

Asha pulled at Suna's hand. Suna lifted her.

---

The compound turned over fast. Sleeping mats rolled. Tin cups clinked into bags. Three weeks of settling gave way in under an hour. Bram took his jacket from the nail by the kitchen door. Mava's afternoon corner by the wall filled with footprints and then with sun.

Suna packed the Mera family's things. There was not much. They had arrived carrying what they could, and they were leaving with less — some of it traded for food during the first week, some of it simply worn past use. The bags smelled of cooking smoke and damp stone and the particular sweetness of mildew that had colonized everything during the monsoon's worst days. She rolled the sleeping mats and tied them with the cord Peyam had braided from kitchen twine during the long afternoons, his fingers working the pattern while he watched the rain. Rimi packed the children's things without being asked. Kael carried Asha's mat, and Asha carried nothing, and this was correct — Asha was four, and four-year-olds carried nothing down mountains except themselves.

Deshi packed her own bag and cinched it shut with one pull. She still had not spoken. The knife-edge Suna had come to expect in her face was absent. She looked as if she had spent the last hour biting down on a seed she could not crack. Once she caught Suna watching, held her eyes for a beat, and went back to folding the spare shawl.

Peyam packed slowly. His knees. He tucked the prayer beads into his shirt pocket and took them out again. He wound them around his wrist and unwound them. On the third try he left them in the pocket. Suna said nothing. There were men who needed a last prayer before the road and men who needed the motions that came before one. Peyam was both.

Jin came to the edge of the Mera family's sleeping space with Melie by the hand. Their bag was already on his back, a single tight bundle for two people. Melie's face still held the puffiness left by private crying. She kept looking at Rimi and then down at her own shoes.

"We'll walk with you," Jin said. The words were steady. The question sat underneath them anyway.

"We're heading south-southwest," Suna said. This was practical, not emotional, and the practicality was the point — it was the language they shared, the language of directions and provisions and logistics, and by speaking it she was saying yes without the weight of saying yes. "We've got enough water for the descent."

"I've got extra," Jin said. He touched the water skin on his belt.

Deshi hauled her bag upright. "Then keep the extra where I can reach it if one of the children slips," she said without looking at him. "Kael runs."

"I will," Jin said.
